 Fig. 10  Microscopic structures in natural plants. (a) Schematic illustration of the structure of wood fibril bundles at different scales, adapted with permission from an online resource.190 (b) Schematic illustration of the hierarchical structure of bamboo, adapted with permission from an online resource,191 and ref. 192–196, Copyright 2011, 2015, and 2018 Springer Nature, 2016 Cabi, and 2019 Frontiers. 
Overall, in terms of some specific requirements regarding the mechanical properties for the MPS products, these wood and bamboo structure inspired innovations are promising. For example, Sun et al.184 proposed a bamboo-like graphitic carbon nanofiber for energy storage applications. The design was inspired by the bamboo structure where there is a periodic distribution of interior holes along with the length and graded pore structure at the cross-section, which would not only enhance the stability with different mechanical deformations but also provide a higher surface area for the access of electrolyte and lower ion-transport resistance. The final device exhibits excellent performance, even when operated under conditions of bending at 90° and twisting at 180°. Recently, Zhu et al.185 reported a bamboo derived biodegradable and biocompatible transparent conductive electrode for PSCs, which exhibits extreme flexibility and light-weight. The bamboo-based bioelectrode PSCs display a PCE of 11.68%, the highest among all biomass-based PSCs. Meanwhile, the flexible device has a highly bendable mechanical stability, maintaining over 70% of its original PCE after 1000 bending cycles at a 4 mm curvature radius. Cai et al.186 developed an efficient HTL consisting of P3HT and bamboo-structured CNTs for PSCs. The addition of bamboo-structured CNTs (1–2 wt%) led to over one-order-of-magnitude higher conductivity and two-order faster interfacial charge transport. These attempts are good examples of how the structured bio-inspired materials could lead to higher mechanical performance of solar cells. Nevertheless, weaving the fiber-like solar cells into fabric patterns by means of bio-inspired pathways (e.g., spider web) or some art forms (e.g., kirigami and origami) might lead to a fabric PV-MPS with unexpected mechanical performance.


3.3.3 Paper-electrode substrate. 
To extend the applied potential in MPS, PSCs with extreme mechanical stretchability are of great interest. This will require the PSCs to have recoverable device performance after various mechanical deformations including stretching, twisting, bending, and delamination. Many prior efforts have mainly focused on bending durability only, leaving stretchability vastly unexplored. Although the aforementioned fiber-structured device and textile-type devices24,29,30 have shown great robustness against various mechanical deformations,175,176,188,197,198 balancing the mechanical performance with PCE is still a technical challenge. The layer-by-layer coating on a metal-fiber backbone needs to be uniform and should have minimized defect density; otherwise, the PCE will drop significantly. The connection of individual fiber-devices into a whole fabric needs to be carefully designed to reduce the longitudinal series resistance for charge transport. For the textile-type device where manufacturing of multiple layers is deposited on a textile, the rough and curved surface of the textile could induce many unwanted interface issues for the whole device. Alternatively, another design could be the “island-bridge” structure (consisting of “device islands” and “wire bridges” connecting these islands, derived from silicon-based stretchable electronics199 and frequently utilized for stretchable systems200), which have already been applied in PSCs. The “island-bridge” design first fabricates the device on a paper-electrode substrate, followed by a papercraft with proper post-cutting (kirigami) or post-folding (origami).201 This enables the PSCs to preserve their initial PCEs under large mechanical deformations such as stretching, bending, and twisting. Jo et al.202 reported that the PSCs based on the kirigami- and origami-strategies demonstrated 400% stretchability and a high areal coverage. The main drawback of the “island-bridge” design is the trade-off between the coverage-area and stretchability. The higher ratio of the “bridge” area will produce a better stretchability, but will lower the total output power due to the smaller area of the “device island” (geometrical fill factor). Fig. 11a(i) shows a solution to this trade-off dilemma. By adopting the origami folding, the bridge can be folded up and stacked in the device region (“island”). This strategy allows an efficient spatial usage (or geometric fill factor) close to 100% (Fig. 11a(ii)). Meanwhile, the stretchability could be maximized by employing different kirigami designs. Li et al.203 also adopted the kirigami-based cutting strategy for a stretchable PSC. Fig. 11b(i) and (ii) show this cutting design, with the out-of-plane deformation possibly being able to sufficiently disperse the stress load, thus increasing the stretchability. The kirigami-based PSCs with an optimal geometric design exhibited high stretchability with a strain of 200%, high twistability with an angle of up to 450°, and high bendability with a radius down to 0.5 mm. Furthermore, the kirigami-based PSCs displayed good mechanical endurance with an invariant PCE after 1000 repetitive stretching, twisting, and bending cycles. Fig. 11b(iii) and (iv) show the photograph of kirigami-based PSCs mounted on a human wrist with accommodation to the stretching deformation. Qi et al.204 also reported kirigami structured PSCs with serpentine interconnects. Fig. 11c shows the schematic diagram and photograph of the stretchable PSCs. The yellow area in Fig. 11c(i) is the gold electrode from which the sub-cells are arranged in arrays. A packaging layer of Eco-flex is further used at both sides of the cell to protect the device. As a result, the obtained devices exhibited a high and stable PCE of 17.68% with a robust mechanical cycling performance of 87% of the initial PCE after 300 cycles of continuous stretching under a large stretching ratio of 80%. The optimized PSC also shows stable efficiency in a harsh environment (80% relative humidity) for 10 days. Overall, research on stretchable device platforms for PSCs is still at the development stage. In addition to the “island-bridge” structure,204 pre-strained wrinkle structure24 and papercraft-inspired approaches202–205 have also been investigated to realize the PSC based portable MPS. This will rely on various state-of-the-art fabrication technologies including photolithography, laser processing, and 3D printing for engineering, optimizing and innovating new device architectures.
 Fig. 11  Adaptation of ‘kirigami’ and ‘origami’ strategies in designing flexible perovskite solar cells. (a) (i) Schematic diagrams of the kirigami/origami approach to achieve a stretchable flexible PSC with high coverage. (ii) Photographs of the stretchable PSC module connected to a fan under sunlight, adapted with permission from ref. 202, Copyright 2019 American Chemical Society. (b) (i) and (ii) Schematic illustration of the structure using the kirigami-based cutting strategy at (i) the initial state and (ii) the stretching state. (iii) Photograph showing the kirigami-based PSC device in the stretching state. (iv) Photograph of kirigami-based PSCs mounted on a human wrist accommodating the stretching deformation, adapted with permission from ref. 203, Copyright 2020 American Chemical Society. (c) (i) Schematic illustration showing the architecture of the kirigami-structured PSC with serpentine interconnect design. (ii) Zoom-in view showing the details of the serpentine interconnect substrate design. (iii) Photograph of the kirigami-structured PSC device with serpentine interconnects, adapted with permission from ref. 204, Copyright 2020 The Royal Society of Chemistry. 

4. Charge transport layer
PSCs are layer-by-layer stacking-structured devices with the core perovskite layer absorbing the light and generating excited electrons and holes, which need to be transferred across multiple interfaces before being collected by the electrodes. Charge transfer layers (CTLs) are responsible for minimizing the charge losses during the interfacial transfer process. In the case of PSCs, multiple HTL and ETL materials have been applied following several selection rules of (i) excellent thermal/photochemical stability; (ii) suitable highest occupied molecular orbital (HOMO)/lowest unoccupied molecular orbital (LUMO) energy level aligning with those of the perovskite; (iii) optimal charge carrier mobility for efficient transport; (iv) good processing ability; (v) good operational phase stability; (vi) low cost and easy attainability; and (vii) proper optical transparency. So far, multiple artificial CTL materials including spiro-OMeTAD,206–210 spiro-like-based derivatives,211–221 carbocyclic small molecules,221–225 nitrogen-, sulfur-, oxygen-, or silicon-containing organics,226–235 metallomacrocycle-based materials,236–238 π-conjugated oligomers, and other inorganic semiconductors239 have been utilized as the HTL for PSCs. Meanwhile, metal oxides (e.g., TiO2, ZnO, SnO2, WO3),240–242 organic n-type materials (PCBM, ICBA243–251), core–shell nanoparticles,252 plasmonic nanoparticles,253–255etc. have been employed as the ETL for PSCs. Optimal CTL materials satisfying all the aforementioned requirements might lead to a new route towards minimized energy losses during interfacial transport in PSCs. Hence, material expansion from these commonly used artificial materials to some novel functional materials that have not been well recognized yet could be a promising research direction, especially if they are cheaper, more abundant and more easily obtainable.
Natural/bio-materials offer such an opportunity. Incorporation of natural bio-semiconductors into electronic, information processing and storage devices, and bio-/artificial-semiconductor interfacing, are growing research areas. New fields at the intersection of synthetic biology and semiconductor technology–semiconductor synthetic biology (SemiSynBio) has been rapidly developing to take advantage of significant efficiency and information processing capabilities from biological systems compared to the best equivalent silicon-based systems. Specifically, deoxyribonucleic acid (DNA) and small cell integrated functional devices, biological/artificial electronics, and biological/artificial coupling systems with high-level automation have been attempted.256 Using natural/bio-semiconducting materials not only broadens the material inventory for seeking a suitable CTL material for ultrahigh-efficiency PSCs, but also offers great opportunities to redefine the architecture and manufacturing of PSCs in a more efficient or intelligent way towards MPS-oriented products.
4.1 DNA

4.1.1 DNA charge transport (CT) mechanism. 
Among those biomaterials of interest, charge transport and relevant charge transfer processes in double helical DNA have attracted huge interest over decades due to their close relevance to the oxidative damage in DNA, which is believed to be related to aging and senility of all living creatures.257,258 In recent decades, research of DNA has discovered long-range charge transport phenomena in DNA and has shown the adaptability in defined nanostructures259 by proper design and synthesis, further stimulating the research interest in DNA serving as a building block for nanoelectronic applications. As shown in Fig. 12a, DNA has four chemical bases: adenine (A), guanine (G), cytosine (C), and thymine (T), where A pairs up with T, while C pairs with G to form the ‘base pair’ units. Each base is also attached to a phosphate molecule and a sugar molecule, forming a cluster called a ‘nucleotide’. Nucleotides are arranged in two long-strands with a spiral form, called a ‘double helix’. The double helix DNA is analogous to a spiral staircase, with the base pairs being the individual steps, while the sugar and phosphate molecules are the vertical railings. Sequencing the base pairs and tuning the pair numbers (coding) of DNA allows a high degree of variability. Each organism has a unique DNA sequence, which details their genetic information. Human DNA consists of about 3 billion base pairs, with 96% similarity to the DNA sequence of great ape species. With the base pair complexity, the charge transfer (CT) behaviour in the DNA double helix can be different. Using different DNA models, multiple mechanisms have been proposed for interpreting the CT along the DNA chains. Fig. 12b summarizes these mechanisms including (i) ballistic (wire-like) transport, (ii) super-exchange, (iii) A-hopping, (iv) G-hopping, and (v) polaronic hopping. The ballistic (wire-like) CT mechanism260 assumes a delocalized π-bond (created by the stacked nucleobases of DNA) through which the injected charge can propagate. Such a ballistic mechanism was proposed by Barton et al.260 to rationalize the observations of a picosecond transfer photoinduced CT between rhodium and ruthenium metallo-complexes intercalated in a DNA stack (Fig. 12b(i)). Super-exchange CT261–263 assumes a well localized donor and acceptor, which occurs when there are large energy gaps between the donor/acceptor and bridge energies. In this way, quantum tunnelling via the states of the intervening bridge dominates the transport, which has been verified by the theoretical prediction of CT in short DNA sequences (Fig. 12b(ii)). Such a CT mechanism is similar to those in proteins, with an exponential decay constant of ∼1 Å−1.263–265 The A-hopping mechanism266 is a multi-step transport which subdivides the transport into two mechanisms: (i) transport between AT pairs and (ii) transport between AT and CG pairs. Charge transport follows an incoherent hopping mechanism among the transport between AT nucleobases but changes into a thermally activated injection when transporting from AT to CG pairs (Fig. 12b(iii)). In G-hopping,267,268 a hole moves between CG nucleobases, mediated by an intervening AT (Fig. 12b(iv)). This model has been verified by multiple atomic force microscopy (AFM) and scanning tunnelling microscopy (STM) experiments showing that the electrical resistance of DNA fragments decreases with an increase in the G-content.269–271 Polaronic hopping (Fig. 12b(v)) is related to the electron/hole cloud polarization that is likely delocalized over several base pairs. This polarization arises due to the redistribution of electron density in the DNA themselves272 and/or due to the interaction with the solvent molecules273 and/or the thermally activated polaronic motion. The polaron model is used to rationalize experimental data272 that there is little change in the radical cation migration efficiency through long distances (10–55 base pair) within the DNA. This will lead to a large electron/hole cloud delocalization over several bases, thereby an overall increased conductivity of the DNA.272
 Fig. 12  DNA and related transport mechanisms. (a) Schematic illustration of the chemical structures of DNA and nucleobases, picture credits to an online resource.274 (b) Charge conduction mechanisms in DNA molecules, adapted with permission from ref. 275, Copyright 2011 Elsevier. (c) Energy level diagram of four basic units, two pairs, DNA macromolecules, and various perovskites. 


4.1.2 DNA buffering solar cells. 
DNA and its derivatives have been applied in organic electronics including organic LEDs,276 organic field-effect transistors (OFETs)277 and OPVs,278,279 by optimizing the interfacial electrical contact between the photoactive layers and the electrodes and/or modifying the micromorphology of the complex organics. Recently, Thomas's group280 found a significant exciton disassociation effect upon interfacing the organic photovoltaic system with the DNA molecules. Several DNA-modified ETLs have been applied in organic solar cells with the device structure of ITO/ETL/P3HT:PC70BM/HTL/Ag (Fig. 13a(i)). By using an ETL of DNA, ZnO-NPs, or ZnO-NPs/DNA, the photocarrier behaviour exhibited different dynamics at the interface, thereby showing different PCEs. Fig. 13a(ii) compares the false color NIR transient absorption (TA) maps for the ITO/ETL/P3HT:PC70BM/MoO3/Ag using an empty ETL or a DNA ETL. Incorporation of the DNA ETL led to an obvious change in the photobleaching at different characteristic wavelengths labelled as PB1, PB2, and PB3 of P3HT (Fig. 13a(iii) and (iv)). Spectral cuts at a probe delay of 800 fs of the TA map in Fig. 13a(iv) show significant variations in PB2 and PB3 features, including a strong blue shift of PB2 from the cell using the ZnO-NPs/DNA ETL. This observation suggests that the ZnO-NPs/DNA has a great photo-optical effect on the conjugation length and intrachain coupling of photocarriers in the P3HT. Overall, insertion of this ultrathin DNA layer between the photoactive layer and electrode leads to a more efficient carrier extraction as evidenced by a lower population of trapped excited exciton states and faster extraction at intermediate timescale decays (5 and 100 ps). It should be noted that, in the above work, DNA can also be chemically doped into a p-type HTL with preferable energy levels better matching those of the photoactive layers. For example, via a redox reaction with hexadecyl trimethyl ammonium chloride (CTMA), the HOMO can be shifted towards −5.4 eV, perfectly aligning with the valence band maximum (VBM) of the MAPbI3 perovskite, while the LUMO of DNA–CTMA can be maintained as high as −1.1 eV. Such a band structure will efficiently block the electron transfer between DNA–CTMA and the MAPbI3 perovskite due to a large Schottky barrier for the electron, but will secure an efficient hole transfer due to the formation of an Ohmic contact. Yusoff et al.281 recently utilized such a DNA–CTMA as the HTL for perovskite solar cells. The well-aligned energy level of the VBM of MAPbI3 with the HOMO of DNA–CTMA minimizes the energy loss for hole transfer and optimizes the magnitude of VOC from 0.90 V to 1.04 V. The LUMO coefficients are located on the thymine and cytosine units of DNA. By tuning the ratio and thus the LUMO to a higher level, the DNA–CTMA HTL could efficiently block electrons, reducing the recombination loss and enhancing both the JSC and PCE from 19.20 to 20.85 mA cm−2 and from 12.49% to 15.86%, respectively. More recently, our group282 also incorporated the hole-extraction facilitated DNA into the grain boundaries (GBs) of a perovskite film (Fig. 13b(i)). A 3 nm-thick DNA layer bridging the neighbouring perovskite crystal grain can facilitate the charge transfer across the GBs, increasing the charge carrier mobility two times from 1.18 × 10−4 cm2 V−1 s−1 to 2.43 × 10−4 cm2 V−1 s−1. As a result, hole extraction throughout the DNA modified device shows a more efficient process with a faster photoluminescence (PL) quenching (Fig. 13b(ii)–(iv)). Consequently, a DNA-modified PSC shows a PCE of 20.63% compared to the value of 18.43% from a reference cell, and the hydrophobic nature of CTMA modified DNA further extends the device's lifetime from the day-scale towards the month-scale.
 Fig. 13  Employment of DNA in organic and perovskite photovoltaics. (a) (i) Schematic illustration of an organic solar cell using DNA as a CTL. (ii) Spectral cuts at a probe delay of 800 fs of the transient absorption of the DNA-incorporated solar cell. (iii) and (iv) Pseudocolor UV-vis transient absorption maps of a solar cell device (iii) with and (iv) without DNA, adapted with permission from ref. 280, Copyright 2018 Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. (b) (i) Schematic illustration of the DNA-integrated perovskite solar cell; (ii) PL emission mapping, (iii) steady state PL spectra, and (iv) time-resolved PL spectra of DNA-integrated MAPbI3 and pristine MAPbI3/hole-transport-layer structures, adapted with permission from ref. 282, Copyright 2019 American Chemical Society. (c) Summary of different types of solar cells being incorporated with DNA: (i) Toschi et al. (ref. 280), (ii) Dagar et al. (ref. 278), (iii) Dagar et al. (ref. 279), (iv) Yusoff et al. (ref. 281), (v) Peng et al. (ref. 283), and (vi) Hou et al. (ref. 282). 
By exploiting its intrinsic molecular conductive nature, DNA can be an excellent integrant in electrically optimizing the properties of functional layers and enhancing the performance of the solar cell device. Fig. 13c summarizes different types of solar cells being incorporated with DNA. It should be noted that PSCs have shown higher PCEs compared to OPVs and represent the optimal platform of applying DNA engineering. Nevertheless, as DNA itself displays complex and multi-hypothetical charge transfer mechanisms, incorporation of DNA into the perovskite system requires an in-depth understanding from the molecular scale to the macroscale for successful device design and operation. More investigations and theoretical models are required to elucidate DNA/perovskite interfacial charge dynamics.

4.2 Fractal neurons

4.2.1 Fractal neurons’ information transport mechanism. 
Fractals are prevalent in nature, in part because of their ability to generate a large surface area within a given volume.143 The fractal leaf vein hierarchy structure, mentioned in the previous section (3.2 Bottom electrode), can efficiently deliver water from the petiole towards the leaf blade. In a broader scale, such a natural fractal design of tree branches allows trees to absorb more sunlight, allows bronchial trees in animals to efficiently transfer oxygen to the bloodstream, and allows the coastlines to sufficiently disperse wave energy from the ocean.284 The nervous system (comprising ∼1011 neurons) in the human brain is another example of fractal geometry as well, with ∼1014 synapses or connections among these brain cells. This indicates an average of 103 connections for each cell through axons reaching out to make the synaptic connections with the dendrites of other neurons. As shown in Fig. 14a, the nervous system is designed in a fractal branching pattern, connecting thousands of neuron's axons and dendrites which allows them to communicate with a large number of neighbouring cells. If the nervous system were designed using a Euclidean geometry, for example in cube-like shapes neatly packed into the brain, each neuron would only connect with at most six other cells, which would not allow the necessary synaptic communication. Overall, the fractal neurons exhibit advantages in delivering, processing and storing information in a more spatially efficient way, which aligns with the fractal geometry structural design. That is, by connecting structural patterns with each other over variable size scales, one can obtain a large surface area and excellent transportability. Accordingly, fractal structural design can efficiently disperse or collect entropy (information), energy, and mass over large temporal and spatial dimensions. Therefore, artificial fractal structures inspired from natural designs (e.g., clouds, coastlines,285 trees, neurons, bronchial trees, blood vessels286) have been widely researched for applied mathematics/physics (e.g., antennas287) and could have great significance when applying them in designing optimized electrodes for solar cells.
 Fig. 14  Fractal design. (a) Photograph showing the neural network, adapted with permission from an online resource.309 (b) (i) Schematic illustration of an idealized morphology of a fractal bulk heterojunction (BHJ) device. (ii) Energy level diagram in the fractal BHJ device, adapted with permission from ref. 291, Copyright 2014 The Royal Society of Chemistry. (c) (i) Photograph of moth eye, adapted with permission from an online resource.310 (ii) and (iii) SEM image showing the moth eye nanostructure, adapted with permission from ref. 311, Copyright 2012 Springer Science and Business Media B.V. (d) (i) SEM image of a moth-eye patterned mesoporous-TiO2 layer (Moth-eye TiO2). (ii) Photograph of normal TiO2 and moth-eye TiO2 on glass substrates. (iii) Comparison of the spatial profiles of the optical absorption per unit volume in the vertical direction of perovskite/flat TiO2 and perovskite/Moth-eye TiO2 at a wavelength of 550 nm, adapted with permission from ref. 300, Copyright 2016 Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. (e) (i) Schematic illustration of a photonic-crystal (PC)-based perovskite solar cell. (ii) Cross-sectional SEM image showing the structure of the PC-based perovskite solar cell. (iii) Experimental (solid lines) and theoretical (dashed lines) reflectance of the PC-based orange (orange lines) and blue-green (dark cyan lines) perovskite solar cells. (iv) CIE 1931 chromaticity space showing the color hues of the PC-based (R) red, (O) orange, (G) green, (BG) blue-green, and (B) blue cells, adapted with permission from ref. 305, Copyright 2015 American Chemical Society. (f) (i) Cross-sectional SEM image showing the structure of a solar cell device based on 2D ‘inverse-opal’ perovskite (IOP) photonic films. (ii) AFM topographic image of IOP photonic films. (iii) The variable-angle reflection spectra of IOP films with different micro-geometries, adapted with permission from ref. 306, Copyright 2016 American Chemical Society. 


4.2.2 Fractal vs. Euclidean design in solar cells. 
The fractal neuron concept is predicted to enhance the charge transport efficiency for solar cells. For example, a branched network is responsible for more efficient light manipulation through sublayer scattering. Meanwhile, interfacial charge transfer at a heterojunction with a proper fractal design could further assist the dissociation of the excitonic state into free charges, which aligns with the “bulk heterojunction (BHJ)” idea in OPVs.288 The structured electrodes are also able to collect the photogenerated charge carriers in a more efficient way. For example, Kay et al.289 constructed Fe2O3 electrodes displaying improved transport properties due to a fractal (‘cauliflower’) structure. Meier et al.290 developed a micro-structured PEDOT:PSS HTL on an Al electrode which enhanced the charge flow and improved the PCE for the solar cell. Recently, Chamousis et al.291 also used fractal electrodes to promote charge separation and extraction from the illuminated photoactive layer. Fig. 14b(i) shows a fractal silver layer with a dendritic topography, coated by a thin PEDOT:PSS HTL used as the photoelectrode. The fractal silver layer on the fluorine-doped tin oxide (FTO) substrate was prepared via electrochemical deposition of silver sulfate (Ag2SO4) under diffusion-limited conditions.292–296 Such a spatial stretching structure benefits the charge flow from the photoactive layer towards the photoelectrode, as illustrated in the energy level diagram in Fig. 14b(ii).


4.2.3 Fractal structure for light manipulation. 
Fractal structures inspired and/or mimicked from examples in nature have been employed in designing high-performance functional structures for various applications.297–299 In solar cell research, these fractal structure designs have been made to manipulate the light for (i) optical reinforcement for higher photocurrent through multiple effects (e.g., anti-reflection effect) and/or (ii) specific coloration for devices through the introduction of ‘artificially colored’ meta-structures. Light manipulation using the anti-reflective function of the structures has been a highlighted part of their applications. For solar cells, optical loss by light reflection at surfaces is responsible for 14% of the total losses. Thus, it is critical to reduce the reflection losses to improve the PCE. Optical reflection occurs at the interface between two media having different optical refractive indices and can be explained by Fresnel's equations.300–302 When light passes through two media of refractive indices n1 and n2, respectively, the reflection (R) at the interface can be predicted using the equation of R = [(n2 − n1)/(n2 + n1)2]. A smaller difference between the refractive indices of the two media will result in less reflection. For 3D structures having subwavelength periods or lengths, the effective refractive index of the system is determined by the volume fraction.303 The eyes of certain species of moths, butterflies, and flies have such a fractal microscopic design with arrays of protuberances which have dimensions smaller than the wavelength of the incident light upon them. Fig. 14c(i) shows a picture of a moth's eye (Cephonodes hylas) with a microscopic view showing the arrays of protuberances (Fig. 14c(ii) and (iii)). Such a feature forms a region with a graded refractive index at an interface (between two media), which can substantially reduce the amount of light that is reflected by the interface. As a result, the reflectance reduction is thought to increase the sensitivity of the visual system of the species. This fractal microstructure also leads to the transparent sections of an insect's wing, giving them a camouflaging effect. On the basis of this fractal moth-eye anti-reflection strategy, an improvement in transparency at the front contact of solar cells has been attempted.
One example in PSCs is the structural modification of the TiO2 ETL in an n–i–p device architecture.304 Kang et al.300 reported utilizing the nanopatterned mp-TiO2 layers with a conical shaped ‘moth-eye’ structure (of a 500 nm-dorm feature) for improving the light harvesting efficiency (LHE). This bio-inspired fractal nanostructure can reduce the reflection at the interface between the mp-TiO2 ETL and the MAPbI3 photoactive layer as the fractal pattern induced optical manipulation which resulted in an antireflective property. Using soft nanoimprinting lithography and polydimethylsiloxane (PDMS) stamping methods, a moth-eye patterned mp-TiO2 layer was successfully obtained, showing a well-ordered periodic 2D hexagonal symmetry (Fig. 14d(i)). The optical enhancement from the moth-eye patterned mp-TiO2 layer was also observed from the photograph of a real film, as can be seen from the colorful surface (light scattered) of the patterned mp-TiO2 film in Fig. 14d(ii). In addition, the finite-difference time-domain (FDTD) method also predicts the optimized optical field distribution in the fractally patterned mp-TiO2 film (Fig. 14d(iii)). As a result, in comparison with the flat layer, PSCs incorporating the moth-eye TiO2 exhibit a 5.3% enhancement of photocurrent and improve the PCE from 15.74% to 17.48%.
The fractal structure in the eye or wings of insects also leads to the strong coloration. This structural color, in contrast to dyes or pigments, originates from the interference of ordered coherent scattering of light waves, which provides the coloration for species. For example, the fractal structure, and the resultant color, in the wings of butterflies is important for camouflage, mating, and warning. Similarly, from the art perspective for the MPS products, PVs with aesthetical versatility, appealing appearance and colorfully vivid features are becoming more and more important in modern life. Beyond the basic power supplier function, multiple products such as building integrated PV and PV built-in clothing also must consider the aesthetics of the end product. In regards to this, Zhang et al.305 developed a multilayered scaffold that can provide optical reflection at certain visible wavelengths and provide the PSC device with structural colors. Fig. 14e(i) and (ii) show the device architecture incorporating a porous photonic crystal (PC) based scaffold with dual functionalities of optical reflection and charge transport. By precisely tuning the reflectance peak of the PC and the absorption from the infiltrated perovskite, vivid iridescences with variable colors ranging across the visible spectrum can be obtained. For example, Fig. 14e(iii) shows the reflectance spectra of an orange (orange lines) and blue-green (dark cyan lines) PSC using the PC scaffold. On further tuning the PC and perovskite nanostructures, colors ranging from red (0.41, 0.34) to orange (0.41, 0.40), green (0.33, 0.41), blue-green (0.29, 0.35), and blue (0.29, 0.30) in the chromaticity space have been obtained for the PSC devices (Fig. 14e(iv)). These PSCs with a wide range of iridescence also exhibit efficient performance with PCEs of up to 8.8% with a blue hue. The use of natural fractal inspired structural colors, rather than utilizing pigments or dyes, as meta-optical materials provides a promising outlook for development of PSCs and their MPS products.
Meng et al.306 also reported vivid colored PSCs using 2D ‘inverse-opal’ perovskite (IOP) photonic films. Typically, the inverse-opal (IO) structured films (film replicas containing colloidal crystals (artificial opals)) are composed of patterns with ordered void arrays surrounded by solid materials in continuous alternations of high and low refractive indices.307 Such a structure affects the light wave propagation and alters the optical properties of the film.308 The 2D IOP film can be fabricated via template-assisted spin-coating processing. Fig. 14f(i) and (ii) show the microscopic structure. Additionally, the IOP film makes the angular dependency of color variation possible, which originated from the optical reflection of the nanostructures (Fig. 14f(iii)). This feature can allow PSCs to be utilized for various applications that require aesthetic functions. Briefly, specifically tuning the IOP micro-geometry and the optical properties of the perovskite (by changing the material composition at the crystallographic A, B and X-sites of the ABX3 halide perovskite), colorful devices with good PCEs (6.7% to 11.2%) have been obtained.

4.3 Bacteriorhodopsin (bR)

4.3.1 Natural role of bR. 
Bacteriorhodopsin (bR) protein extracted from Halobacterium salinarum has been broadly utilized in multiple biohybrid electronics known as “bioelectronics” that merges current electronic techniques with biology.312 The bR (playing the role of a light-driven proton pump in the purple membrane (PM) of the archaea Halobacterium salinarum) consists of seven-transmembrane (7TM) helices and a retinal chromophore covalently bound in the central region through a protonated Schiff base to a lysine residue (Fig. 15a(i)). The PM (Fig. 15a(ii) and (iii)) is composed of a 2D hexagonal crystal with an ∼6.2 nm unit cell (containing the bR trimer and lipid molecules as the intertrimer medium). The photocycle in bR can be initiated by the photon absorption at the covalently bound chromophore retinal which leads to a series of conformational changes within the protein and ultimately to the proton transport across the cell membrane. Such a photon-activated mechanism, along with the ability of bR to regenerate, makes it possible to develop bR-based nano-optoelectronics.313 For example, the aligned bR PM film on ITO has shown an efficient and stable photocurrent response.314 Early attempts of utilization of bR as a light-activated component in PV cells exhibited 9.73 mV cm−2 and 41.7 mV cm−2 in dry and aqueous systems, respectively.315 Although there have been many successful cases of bR-based biohybrid electronics (which have been well reviewed elsewhere312), practical utilization of these biomolecular-based components in electronics requires a detailed understanding of the coupling of these individual bR molecules to the macroscopic surfaces of electrodes, as well as the evolutions in the electronic environment under dynamic (nonequilibrium) conditions.316 Prior research has revealed that bR can be fully functional over a wide range of temperatures and pH, while the conductivity of bR could be separated into two regimes, determined by various charge transport mechanisms.317–319 At lower temperatures (T < 250 K), the electrical conductance is dominated by the electron tunneling mechanism which switches to the electron hopping mechanism as the temperature increases.317 The electron hopping mechanism will be affected by the removal of the retinal chromophore within the bR. The interaction intensity between the bR protein and the electrode substrate can also affect the transport mechanism.318
 Fig. 15  Bacteriorhodopsin (bR) related CTL application in PVs. (a) (i) Schematic illustration of the bacteriorhodopsin (bR) in archaea Halobacterium salinarum, adapted with permission from an online resource.338 (ii) Unit cell structure and (iii) top view of multiple unit cells of the purple membrane (PM) containing bR trimer and lipid molecules, adapted with permission from online resources.339,340 (b) (i) Energy level diagram of charge carrier injection in the bR/TiO2 photoanode cell. (ii) SEM image of the TiO2 nanotube arrays with the inset showing the cross-sectional view, adapted with permission from ref. 313, Copyright 2011 The Royal Society of Chemistry. (c) (i) The equivalent energy level diagram of the photochemical reaction in a bR-integrated photovoltaic stacking system. (ii) Schematic illustration showing the structure of the bR/AuNPs heterogeneous multilayers, adapted with permission from ref. 320, Copyright 2015 Elsevier. (d) (i) Energy level diagram of a bR-integrated perovskite solar cell (BPSC). (ii) Schematic illustration of the architecture of the BPSC, adapted with permission from ref. 328, Copyright 2019 American Chemical Society. 


4.3.2 Applied role of bR. 
As the bR is responsible for a series of photochemical reactions ultimately leading to the protons pumping across the membrane, PVs can utilize each individual reaction to promote either efficient photon absorption or charge transport. Hence, different operating mechanisms in regards to bR being applied in PVs have been reported. Table 4 summarizes the studies that used bR to promote charge transport in related optoelectronics. For example, Allam et al.313 reported on the assembly and utilization of a bR/TiO2 nanotube array as the photoelectrode in photoelectrochemical applications. The ∼7 μm thick nanotube array of TiO2 films was fabricated by room-temperature anodization of Ti-foil in formamide electrolytes containing NH4F, followed by sensitization with bR. This nanotube array of TiO2 enables sufficient attachment of bR at the surface (Fig. 15b(ii)). By using the bR/TiO2 hybrid photoelectrodes for photoelectrochemical water splitting, the hybrid electrodes obtained an increased photocurrent density of 0.65 mA cm−2 (under AM 1.5 illumination), about 50% improvement compared to the pure TiO2 nanotubes (0.43 mA cm−2) fabricated under the same conditions. The improvement is due to the efficient bR-assisted electron transfer from the electrolyte to the photoanode of TiO2. Fig. 15b(i) shows the energy level diagram and the mechanism of electron injection to the bR/TiO2 photoanode in the presence of a redox I3−/I− electrolyte solution. The bR has a LUMO and a HOMO energy level of −3.8 and −5.4 eV (based on surface photovoltage spectroscopy (SPS314)), respectively. TiO2 has a conduction band maximum (CBM) of −4.2 eV, which allows an efficient electron injection from bR to TiO2 (Fig. 15b(i)). In the presence of the redox electrolyte (0.02 M I3−/I−), an electron can be captured by bR and excited from the HOMO to the LUMO upon illumination, which is then expected to travel to the TiO2 photoanode. Guo et al.320 also proposed a bR/metal heterogeneous structure for efficient charge transfer, which also leads to a novel PV stack system. On the optical side, using delicate, well-patterned structures to maximize the light absorption is one of the most widely used strategies in nature. For example, in the leaf cell, as the core organ for photosynthesis, Granum321 consists of a stack of 10–100 thylakoids containing pigments and electron-acceptors. When the light stimulates the antenna pigment in the thylakoids, electrons can be excited and transferred via the photoelectric conversion process. The stacking design of thylakoids provides a large surface area which allows highly efficient solar radiation capture. It has been reported that, by mimicking this stacking design through construction of TiO2 nanosheets templated by graphene, the photoelectric response can be enhanced ∼20 times.322 The bR and its surrounding purple membrane also have a 2D crystalline feature (Fig. 15a(iii)), making it possible to stack with a metal to form a laminar electrode capable of more efficient radiation capture. This has been achieved by constructing bR/Au NPs heterogeneous multilayers for a photovoltaic stacking system (Fig. 15c(ii)), where the bR layers are inserted between AuNP layers. The bR layers serve as the photon acceptor and the Au NP layers can further enhance the photocurrent because of their native surface plasmon field effect. Fig. 15c(i) illustrates the equivalent energy level diagram of the photochemical reaction in these stacks. When a continuous incident light (350–800 nm) is shed on the device, the excited state can be pumped by AuNPs leading to the emission of ∼520 nm light. The 520 nm light is then absorbed by bR, giving rise to a photocycle starting from the B570-state to the J600-state, and then passes to the M412-state in bR. It should be noted that the blue light is also contained in the stimulated light source, which can directly affect the bR's ability to accelerate the decay of the immediately formed M412-state in bR.323 This provides a shorter time pathway II (light activates the M412 to an excited state Me,324 which can facilitate a quicker decay to the B state) to complete the typical photocycle, leading to a ‘bypass photocycle model’ in bR. With the stacking of Au NP layers, the photocycle pathway of the partial bR molecule will take this (‘bypass’ short-cut) pathway II, which represents a more efficient and accelerated photocycle for bR. For PV applications, bR has been employed as a photosensitizer in bio-sensitized solar cells (BSSCs), where the bR proteins are attached on a TiO2 film. These proteins can be photo-excited and transfer the excited states to the TiO2 photoanode, and also refill the electrons from the redox reaction with the electrolyte. Thavasi et al.325 built the first BSSC using bR as a light harvester, being adsorbed at the TiO2 electrode, exhibiting a VOC of 0.35 V and a JSC of 0.09 mA cm−2 under an illumination of 40 mW cm−2. Following this, Mohammadpour et al.326 and Jeganathan et al.327 fabricated BSSCs based on the TiO2/bR electrode and the devices displayed PCEs of 0.35% and 0.49% under AM 1.5, respectively. More recently, Das et al.328 reported a bR assisted photocycle in PSCs. The bR can be inserted between the TiO2 ETL and the perovskite photoactive layer, which implements the energy transfer step involving photon absorption and the Förster resonance energy transfer (FRET) process. Fig. 15d shows the energy level diagram (Fig. 15d(i)) and the schematic illustration of the corresponding PSC device (Fig. 15d(ii)). The light energy conversion can be achieved through the transfer of the photogenerated electrons from the perovskite to the bR molecules through a FRET process.329–331 The photogenerated electrons can be subsequently transferred to the TiO2 ETL, driven by the energy level difference between bR and TiO2.332,333 This bR bridge between the perovskite and TiO2 is further verified by the observation of reduced interfacial charge recombination. As a result, the bR modified PSC exhibits a higher PCE of 17.02% compared to that of 14.59% from a reference blank device.

Overall, the bR photocycle effect on photocurrent enhancement provides an additional mechanism for tuning the interface between the photoactive layer and the photoelectrode. Considering the control role of a proton ‘gate’ (bR's original role in creatures, as shown in Fig. 15a) and the photocycle process of bR, there could be two ways to export the optical signals of bR. One is to design and construct a photocell system joined by two half-cells, which can produce the proton gradient difference between the two half-cells, i.e., a proton ‘gate’. The other one is to couple the proton with electronic carriers, inducing the displacement and recombination of the charged carriers during the bR photocycle, resulting in the generation of a differential photovoltage. The ongoing research using bR incorporated hybrid structures for enhancing the photocurrent or frequency response is expected to be applied in the field of biological electronics with potential applications in medical electronics such as bR-based immunosensors334 and bR-based artificial retinal prostheses.335 Nevertheless, in solar cells, bR incorporation taking advantage of its ‘gate’ and photocycle role in complex with perovskite crystals still remains an untapped research area, with the energy/mass transfer mechanism at the bR/perovskite interface being a particularly interesting research topic. In fact, many other dyes besides bR have shown unusual energy transfer mechanisms, which has led to several interesting subtopics such as triplet–triplet annihilation (TTA) upconversion336 and singlet fission (SF)337 that can bring new insights for reducing the thermodynamic losses in PSCs and hypothetical foundations for exceeding the Shockley–Queisser (SQ) limit of solar cells.

4.4 Other natural materials
Natural materials including biological molecules with conjugated structures or radical features can be good charge transport materials for electronic applications. Particularly, for the π-conjugated natural pigments such as chlorins (a specific type of porphyrin which is in the form of Mg2+ complexes found as the green photosynthetic pigments in plants (chlorophylls)), the energies of frontier molecular orbitals (i.e., HOMO and LUMO) and the distribution of the orbitals (i.e., DOS) in these molecules play crucial roles in intra- and inter-molecular charge transport and are also relevant to the light absorption/emission, charge injection/extraction/trapping and electron pushing/pulling or donating/accepting effects. These π-conjugated features in those artificially synthesized semiconducting polymers/small organic molecules enable their optoelectronic properties and make them widely applied in fields such as OPVs and OLEDs. Natural pigments also have such a conjugated structure, which makes them good candidates for CTL materials in PSCs.

4.4.1 Porphyrins. 
Porphyrins, derived from the Greek word ‘porphyros’ meaning ‘purple’, are compounds of cyclic tetrapyrroles with intense colors. In nature, porphyrins are the parent members of pigments in various species and serve key roles in various vital processes such as metabolism and photosynthesis. For example, the heme has a porphyrin ring (Fig. 16a), where the iron center (Fe2+) in the porphyrin ring can coordinate with the oxygen (O2) serving as the oxygen carrier. Hence the blood cell containing heme can carry oxygen from the lungs to various tissues. In fact, 70% of the body's iron is present in red blood cells as hemoglobin, composed of iron-porphyrin heme groups, resulting in the red color of human blood (color of Fe2+; this is different from the blue color of the blood of spiders and octopuses which contains Cu2+). Hemes are also crucial in enzymes (e.g., liver enzymes metabolizing drugs), catalases (protecting cells from oxidative damage), or electron transfer proteins.346 Another example of a natural porphyrin complex is found in the chloroplasts of algae and plants, i.e., the chlorophyll, a green pigment critical in photon absorption, resonance energy transfer and photoexcited charge separation in photosynthesis. Chlorophyll molecules are embedded in the thylakoid membranes of chloroplasts in photosystems (Fig. 16b) and are well arranged so that light harvesting and transportation (>95% transfer efficiency) could maximize the efficiency in such a small system. The high energy transfer efficiency is realized by the unique property of chlorophyll (incorporated with Mg2+ in the porphyrin ring) that the absorption and emission spectra overlap exceptionally well, so the energy transfer to nearby molecules can favorably follow the Förster (resonance) mechanism. Following this, the photon energy can be harvested by chlorophyll and then transferred among ∼100 chlorophyll molecules through this resonance mechanism. As a result, the overall energy transfer process, from photon absorption to arrival at the reaction center, is less than 10 ps, which is significantly faster than the chlorophyll fluorescence lifetime of 5 ns (in this way the fluorescence loss is negligible). Replacing Mg2+ with other metals will not only change the absorption and emission spectra, inducing less efficient resonance energy transfer, but also competitively quench the chlorophyll's excited state (through a spin–orbit coupling or ‘heavy atom effect’) and reform the ground state. Therefore, the Mg2+ chlorophyll is designed by nature to maximize the efficiency of photosynthesis. Additionally, other porphyrin-derived natural pigments are found in cobalamin (vitamin B12 source as shown in Fig. 16c, which is typically involved in the metabolism of every cell in the human body, affecting DNA synthesis and regulation as well as fatty acid and amino acid metabolism), biliverdin and protoporphyrin (Fig. 16d, found in thin regions of eggshells and proposed to strengthen the egg shell347). Overall, the porphyrin-based materials with relatively high chemical stability have shown an enormous application range, including chemical usage (oxidation, atom transfer, and electron transfer catalysts), advanced functional devices (CO2 reduction, light harvesting, information storage, and chemosensors), medical applications (photochemotherapy for cancer treatment), and imaging techniques as well (used as fluorescent/photoacoustic imaging dyes in the biomedical field).
 Fig. 16  Porphyrins and their derivatives. (a) Schematic illustration of a (i) blood cell, (ii) molecular structure of hemoglobin and (iii) chemical structure of the heme group. Adapted with permission from online resources.348,349 (b) (i) Microscopic image of Plagiomnium affine laminazellen showing the (ii) chlorophyll in the cell, schematic illustration of the chloroplast structure, and (iii) chemical structure of chlorophyll. Adapted with permission from an online resource.350 (c) Images of foods rich in vitamin B12 (left) and chemical structure of cobalamin (right), adapted with permission from an online resource.351 (d) Image of eggshells of different avian lineages showing varying color and pigment patterns (left), and chemical structures of biliverdin and protoporphyrin XI (right), adapted with permission from ref. 352, Copyright 2016 Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. 
Considering their low oxidation potential and high charge transport capabilities, porphyrins are considered to be efficient CTLs in PVs. Because of the large structural tuning capacity, porphyrin materials could have high versatility in terms of electrochemical, photophysical and charge-transport properties, making them good CTL candidates for perovskite materials of highly tunable energy levels. In addition, porphyrins have excellent thermal stability and high hole mobility due to the strong π–π stacking and intermolecular charge transfer of the porphyrin film (in fact the porphyrin materials have been applied as electron donors in BHJ OPVs353). These jointly secure their initial success in PSCs. Table 5 summarizes prior examples of using porphyrin materials as HTLs in PSCs. In 2016, Chou et al.354 reported the first porphyrin based HTL (zinc porphyrins: Y2 and Y2A2) for PSCs. As shown in Fig. 17a, Y2 and Y2A2 consist of a meso-5,15-bis(ethynylaniline)porphyrin backbone bearing butyl (Y2) or dodecyl (Y2A2) chains, with two facing alkoxyphenyl groups in the other two opposite meso positions. The design benefits the electronic communication between the porphyrin core and the donor amine group. As a result, the Y2 and Y2A2 films exhibit a well-matched HOMO of −5.22 eV (close to the CBM of −5.4 eV of MAPbI3) and a high hole mobility of 10−4 cm2 V−1 s−1, resulting in a PCE of 16.60% (comparable to that of 18.03% from a reference cell using spiro-OMeTAD). Chen et al.355 reported the 5,10,15,20-tetrakis{4-[N,N-di(4-methoxylphenyl)amino-phenyl]}-porphyrin with Zn(II) and Cu(II) being centered in the porphyrin ring (abbreviated as ‘ZnP’ and ‘CuP’ in Fig. 17b). Density functional theory (DFT) results show that the four meso-substituted triarylamines surrounding the porphyrin ring are nearly perpendicular to the porphyrin due to steric hindrance, giving a twisted spatial structure. Both the porphyrin core and the triarylamine contribute to the HOMO DOS distribution of ZnP and CuP, while the LUMO is only determined by the porphyrin ring. Compared to ZnP (−5.29 eV), CuP has a lower HOMO of −5.37 eV. The corresponding film exhibits a similar hole mobility of about 3 × 10−4 cm2 V−1 s−1, nearly two-fold higher than that of spiro-OMeTAD (1.58 × 10−4 cm2 V−1 s−1). A ZnP based PSC exhibits a PCE of 17.78% compared to the spiro-OMeTAD reference (18.59%). For the HTL application in solar cells, the morphological features of the film, such as pin-hole, uniformity, surface roughness, and porous density, as well as their effects on mobility, electrical conductivity and slight energy-level shifting, are crucial to the final device performance. Re-engineering the molecular structure of porphyrins to optimize the intermolecular interaction and their stacking in the final film can be a shortcut. Chiang et al.356 extended the coplanarity of porphyrins by using the dimeric porphyrin conjugate structure.357–359 The resultant porphyrin dimers WT3 and YR3 were composed of ethynylaniline electron-donating moieties attached at the lateral meso-positions of the porphyrin (Fig. 17c). The enhanced coplanarity enables a better intermolecular interaction and a higher PCE of 19.44% from the WT3 based device (compared to that of 18.62% from the spiro-OMeTAD reference device). Kang et al.360 employed three different substituted electron-donating triarylamine (TAA) groups to extend the π-conjugation of the molecule (coded as SGT-061, SGT-062, and SGT-063 with their structures being shown in Fig. 17d). These molecules consist of a coplanar porphyrin core and different TAA bulky donor groups which possess a larger dihedral angle between the alkoxy-substituted phenyl ring and the donor TAA. This spatial effect gives rise to a strong interference for the dense molecular arrangement and a modulation on the π–π intermolecular stacking. Consequently, the SGT-061 with a less bulky donor group and a tighter molecular stacking exhibits a higher hole mobility of 2.60 × 10−5 cm2 V−1 s−1 than those of SGT-062 (1.28 × 10−5 cm2 V−1 s−1) and SGT-063 (8.01 × 10−6 cm2 V−1 s−1). Although the device exhibits a relatively lower PCE of 9.0–12.6% (compared to the value of 16.6% from the spiro-OMeTAD-based reference cell) due to the low hole mobility, the rule of “the less bulky, the easier molecular stacking” is verified in practice. Overall the molecular stacking of the porphyrin materials plays the most important role in their HTL films for PSCs. Besides the modulation on such a coplanarity, the intermolecular stacking and the consequent film morphologies can also be influenced by the functional moieties surrounding the core porphyrin ring. For example, Lee et al.361 designed a series of novel zinc porphyrins (PZn) with different diphenyl-2-pyridylamine (DPPA) moieties surrounding the porphyrin cores. These DPPA moieties had electron deficient pyridine bridging the core porphyrin (Fig. 17e), so the energy level and spatial interaction of adjacent molecules could be adjusted in certain levels. The DPPA group also contributes to the oriented stacking of the molecules, as the 2D grazing-incidence X-ray diffraction (2D-GIXD) revealed that the PZn–DPPA film showed enhanced face-on π–π stacking compared to the PZn–TPA film (with the (010) out-of-plane (Qz) peak at around 1.70 Å−1 corresponding to an intermolecular π–π distance of ca. 3.70 Å). This improved face-on π–π stacking might be the reason for the promoted vertical charge transport, as observed in the mobility results. Consequently, the PZn–DPPA has the lowest HOMO of −5.36 eV and a decent hole mobility of 4.10 × 10−4 cm2 V−1 s−1, delivering a PCE of 14.11%, comparable to the value of 14.61% from the spiro-OMeTAD-based reference device. Moreover, the modulation of the porphyrin based HTL can also be electronically changed through the adjustment of the functional groups. This will change the HOMO, for example, and affect the hole extraction at the perovskite/HTL interface. It has been well documented in OPVs that the incorporation of strong electron-withdrawing elements such as fluorine (F) can mitigate the π-electron cloud and downshift the energy level to a certain degree. Azmi et al.362 employed this strategy to further decrease the HOMO energy level of the porphyrin HTL to make an Ohmic contact between the perovskite and HTL. Specifically, Zn porphyrin (PZn)-based materials with four fluorinated triphenylamine (FTPA) substituents (PZn–2FTPA and PZn–3FTPA in Fig. 17f) have been synthesized. As expected, the PZn–2FTPA displayed a lower HOMO of −5.37 eV compared to −5.14 eV from the non-fluorinated PZn–TPA. The PZn–3FTPA displayed a comparable hole mobility of 3.91 × 10−4 cm2 V−1 s−1 and a higher PCE of 18.85% compared to the values of 3.37 × 10−4 cm2 V−1 s−1 and 16.37%, respectively, for the non-fluorinated PZn–TPA device. Additional studies on engineering of the molecular structure of porphyrin-based HTLs have also been reported with the corresponding molecular structures being shown in Fig. 17g and h and the device performance parameters listed in Table 5. Beyond the scope of structural engineering of the porphyrin material, the oxidation state of the porphyrin HTL can tune the carrier concentration and consequently the electrical conductivity. For example, Cao et al.363 developed dual valent Co(II)/(III) mixed incorporation into the porphyrin film. The Co(II)/Co(III) pair is typically known as one of the best combinations for redox processes, which has been used as an electrolyte in DSSCs.364–366 A mixture of Co(II)P and Co(III)P363 (Fig. 17i) has been used as the HTL for PSCs as well. Normally the methoxy-substituted triphenylamine group in most above-mentioned porphyrin HTLs is responsible for the high solubilities in common organic solvents and the extended π-conjugation is responsible for efficient charge transport. The additional methoxy-substituted oxyalkyl chain attached at the triphenylamine group can further tune the solubility of the whole porphyrin molecule.
In addition, the centered Co(II) and Co(III) serve as a redox couple to tune the oxidation level of the HTL. Consequently, both Co(II)P and Co(III)P HTLs exhibit similar hole mobilities to that of spiro-OMeTAD but slightly lower HOMO levels of −5.30 and −5.28 eV, respectively. The Co(II)P/Co(III)P mixed HTL displayed a PCE of 20.5% compared to that of ∼19% from the spiro-OMeTAD based reference cell. In addition to the role of charge transfer in PSCs, the porphyrin materials can also serve as a dual functional layer that simultaneously harvests photons, excites the charges and transports these excited charges. Gao et al.367 reported a hybrid PV cell composed of a NIR-absorbing BHJ layer on top of the perovskite. The BHJ layer containing a mixture of DPPEZnP-TSEH and PCBM not only displayed a broad photoresponse to over 800 nm but also possessed suitable energy levels and a high mobility to afford a high PCE of 19.02%. The NIR photon usage for PV conversion by the top BHJ layer is verified by the extended EQE as well as the transient absorption study. Above all, various examples referring to the changes in the meso-substitution of tetraaryl-porphyrin (such as variations of the substituents and metal centers) have been demonstrated and discussed, which can have different impacts on the energy levels, absorption features, intermolecular interaction/stacking and its effect on the final film morphology as well as electrical properties. Additional versatility of porphyrin derivatives by reforming the cyclic tetrapyrrolic ring, such as other tetrapyrrole-based molecules (e.g., chlorins (Chl),368 bacteriochlorins (BChl),369 Zn(II)chlorophyll derivatives with BChl, Chl, and Por macrocycles,370 and other core-modified porphyrins371), has been studied to use them as potential HTLs for PSCs. Fig. 18 compares their molecular structures and relative performance as the HTL in PSCs. Although there have been a wide range of artificial porphyrin derivatives synthesized and employed in PV cells, the principle motif of using these porphyrin HTLs is to transfer the charge carriers through their π-conjugated electronic structure. This is relatively superficial in comparison to their delicate role in nature. For example, the chlorophyll in leaf cells is constructed in such a design that the energy transfer strictly follows the highly efficient Förster resonance mechanism. Instead of directly utilizing certain biomaterials as a disordered film in devices, delicately arranging the biomaterials into a subsystem (having an ordered structure or a delicately designed structure to evoke a highly efficient working mechanism) with maximal abilities of charge transfer might be a better solution.

4.4.2 Other natural conjugates. 
The porphyrins and analogous derivatives discussed above represent the most widely used materials in PVs. Particularly, their optical response featuring two absorbance bands in visible and NIR regions also enables their applications as photoactive materials (dyes) in DSSCs. The high hole mobility also benefits their ‘electron donor’ role in the BHJ blend in OPVs. Apart from these porphyrins and their analogous derivatives, natural pigments with aromatic fused π-conjugated structures can also be potential CTL materials in PSCs. Similar to porphyrin derivatives, most of them have been applied as photoactive dyes in DSSCs. Table 6 summarizes some of the natural conjugates, their corresponding properties and roles in nature. Anthocyanins, a type of flavonoid (a class of compounds with antioxidant effects), are the pigments that give different shades of red, purple, and blue to plants. Anthocyanins have higher chemical reactivity than other flavonoid classes, due to their specific pyrylium nucleus (C-ring) (Fig. 19a). To exemplify, anthocyanins are also ubiquitous water-soluble pigments having important roles in the physiology of plants (repelling herbivores and parasites,374 attracting pollinators and seed dispersers,375 and protecting plants against biotic and abiotic stresses376) and humans (antioxidant,377 anti-inflammatory,378 neuroprotective,379 and anti-diabetic properties380). Chemically, anthocyanins are weak diacids, nucleophiles, and hard and soft electrophiles, with a high tendency to develop π–π stacking interactions and binding with hard metal ions.381 The π–π stacking can facilitate the film to have higher intermolecular charge transfer, and the binding ability to metal ions can potentially passivate lattice defects in perovskites. They also exhibit the typical chemical properties of polyphenols, including an electron donor property and affinity with proteins. Their biological role in terms of transformation and catabolism in humans and effects on health is well-reviewed elsewhere.381 Carotenoids, the tetraterpene pigments with yellow, orange, and red colors (Fig. 19b), are widely distributed in photosynthetic bacteria, certain species of fungi and archaea, algae, plants, and animals, giving the characteristic color to pumpkins, carrots, corn, tomatoes, canaries, flamingos, salmon, lobster, shrimp, and daffodils.382 Carotenoids are conjugated double bond compounds with recurring isoprene units, which are responsible for the π-electron delocalization along the backbone and thereby a certain level of electrical conductance. All carotenoids are highly lipophilic and exhibit low solubility in water, making them potential materials for introducing hydrophobicity to the moisture-sensitive perovskite materials in PSCs. Their conjugated nature also makes them antioxidants (inactivate the reactive oxygen species and oxidative damage, which is essential to the immune system), attractants, and UV attenuators.383 Recently, carotenoids have received growing attention because of their therapeutic values and health benefits, as well as their applications in the perfume and food industries.384,385 Mangostin386 is the major xanthone from Garcinia mangostana (whose fruits are referred to as the ‘queen of fruits’ in Thailand387,388) and has been utilized as the starting material for semisynthetic preparation of multiple biologically active derivatives. Its structure is shown in Fig. 19c, with the presence of multiple instances of including two prenyl chains, three phenolic hydroxyl groups and two unsubstituted aromatic carbons. Mangostin displays chemopreventive properties in medical applications. In PV applications, mangostin is used as the dye in DSSCs (exhibiting a relatively low PCE of 2.63%).389 Rutin (3,3′,4′,5,7-pentahydroxyflavone-3-rhamnoglucoside, shown in Fig. 19d) is a flavonoid pigment in medicinal plants (usually found in sources such as buckwheat, Japanese pagoda tree, Eucalyptus, hawthorn, lime tree flowers, elder flowers, etc.) and is used to make medicines (responsible for various pharmacological qualities including antioxidant, anticarcinogenic, cytoprotective, vasoprotective, neuroprotective and cardioprotective properties). As the natural antioxidant, rutin has been reported as the reducing agent in synthesis of Ag NPs.390,391 The rutin-modified Ag NPs can be used as a chromogenic probe for metal ions in aqueous environments.392 Elseman et al.393 reported the strategy of silver chelation on rutin as an additive for increasing the hole conductivity in the HTL. The rutin-modified Ag NP blended PHPT-py HTL led to a PCE of 21.1% for the PSC, while the reference cell using the typical spiro-OMeTAD HTL has a PCE of only 20.8%. This is due to the re-oxidation effect by rutin–Ag NPs on the PHPT-py HTL, enhancing the electrical conductance of the HTL. The deoxidized rutin can be easily oxidized by oxygen and these oxidized states of rutin can be further reduced by the PHPT-py HTL. Hence, the catalytic role of rutin–Ag NPs improves the properties of the PHPT-py HTL. Meanwhile the chelation-like rutin–Ag NP complex restricts the migration of the halide group in the perovskite and consequently improves the operational stability of the PSC device. Overall, the rutin–Ag NPs could be a promising additive to the existing HTL materials in PSCs. Anthocyanins (a type of flavonoid with antioxidant effects; the structure is shown in Fig. 19e) are found in vibrantly colored fruits, leaves, and flowers capable of attaching to transition metal oxides (TMOs) such as TiO2 surface which has been reported as the photo-sensitizer in DSSCs.394,395 Although anthocyanin pigments are abundant in nature, isolated anthocyanins are highly degradable,396 depending on several factors such as pH, storage temperature, and sunlight exposure.397Betalains (Fig. 19f) are a class of yellow and red tyrosine-derived pigments found in the cactus Mammillaria flower petals, red beets, Swiss chard, Amaranthus caudatus, etc. Betalains exhibit many attractive properties including antioxidant, anti-cancer, anti-lipidemic and antimicrobial properties.398 Betalains are water soluble with functional –OH and –COOH groups which can make it easy to attach TMOs. Other commonly used natural dyes as photon absorbers in DSSCs include the chain-like molecules such as β-carotene (non-polar compound and highly lipophilic, shown in Fig. 19g(i)), lutein (lipophilic and generally insoluble in water, shown in Fig. 19g(ii)), violaxanthin (a natural xanthophyll pigment, shown in Fig. 19g(iii)), and neoxanthin (a carotenoid and xanthophyll, shown in Fig. 19g(iv)).399 There are a wide range of natural pigment materials where the large π-electron conjugation enables visible light absorption and hence the coloring properties of these materials. The π-electron conjugation and delocalization also make it possible for charge transport in a certain range. Considering their application as CTLs in PSCs, an intermolecular hopping mechanism must be feasible, requiring close intermolecular packing of these natural pigments within the thin film. Additionally, intentional doping to increase the charge carrier density or improve other mechanisms such as radical assisted transport and/or organic transport can be possible routes towards highly efficient CTLs applied in PSCs. Although the application of these natural conjugates as CTLs in PSCs remains unexplored, their potentially high charge carrier mobility, tunable energy level, good solution-processability and benefits compared to artificial materials (e.g., low-cost, abundance in supply, and reliable stability) may trigger new protocols for more efficient CTLs for PSCs.
