In terms of perovskites, one challenge in this material is its high chemical activity with oxygen and moisture in air. Placing a halide perovskite thin film in the ambient atmosphere will introduce a gradual surface degradation (chemical impurities, surface traps, etc.) that is harmful for their use in PSCs.476,477 Removing this already degraded surface layer inspired by the ecdysis and moulting of animals (Fig. 24d) could be a potential solution. In fact, the concept of ‘surface layer removal to obtain a high-quality fresh layer’ has been widely used chemically and physically in manufacturing (e.g., surface cleaning of monocrystalline silicon wafers for chip manufacturing)478 and in research (e.g., mechanical exfoliation of 2D materials for obtaining a fresh surface479,480). For a perovskite crystal, the surface trap has significant harmful impacts on the interfacial contact. Fig. 24e shows a cross-sectional SEM image of a perovskite crystal with poor surface morphology. Properly polishing the top surface with techniques such as laser polishing or other fine lapping may help to passivate those unwanted surface defects. To prevent surface degradation of halide perovskite films, an alternative pathway could be a quick surface passivation before reaction with air. Like the wound healing process at the epidermis in soft tissues of the invertebrates, covering the reactive surface with a clotting-like ultrathin layer may be helpful in inhibiting the reaction with ambient harmful agents. This spontaneous self-surface-protection could be conceptualized either by introducing specific additives in the perovskite that can spontaneously form a robust top protecting layer on the halide perovskite481 (will be discussed in Section 5.3 – Bio-additives) or by intentionally coating a capping layer on top of the wet halide perovskite film before its reaction with air.482 Protective skin and an outer shell are widely used strategies in nature. Fig. 24f shows the examples of cell walls of plants that can protect the cell, skin epidermis that can protect the inner tissues, the exoskeleton of insects that can provide protection against harsh environmental stimuli, and marine collagens that can protect marine organisms. In comparison, for the research of perovskite solar cells, different protection strategies have been proposed (Fig. 24f). For example, a core–shell structure could protect the core perovskite lattice, an ultrathin organic coating layer on the grain could protect the crystal grain of the perovskite, and a 2D capping layer could protect the 3D perovskite layer underneath. This is particularly important for perovskite solar cells, where the surface traps account for a significant loss. Particularly in single-crystalline perovskite solar cells, since the inner bulk region is having no boundaries, surface traps could lead to a serious issue in contact with the CTL or electrodes. Passivating these surface traps or delaminating the top-rough ‘skin’ to induce a smooth inner surface during device assembly is the next hurdle to overcome. This may require sophisticated manufacturing techniques such as laser-annealing, laser polishing and flashing lamp annealing techniques.


5.3.2 Self-healing in PSCs. 
PSCs targeted for MPS applications may have soft and deformable attributes. However, soft elastic materials typically used for PSCs and other electronics are susceptible to puncturing and tearing, leading to mechanical failures, performance losses, and even the lead leakage from the perovskite layer. This potential failure greatly limits the use of MPS in applications such as autonomous field robotics or remote mission (e.g., spatial mission) where repairs are challenging or even impossible. Hence, soft MPS which are self-healable and functionally repairable like natural skin or nervous tissue are of great interest. In a higher-level field view of artificial self-healing materials, many nature-inspired mechanisms and materials have been proposed. These include semiconducting polymeric networks,540–542 recoverable polymers,543 ionic hydrogels,544 liquid metal (LM) microfluidics,545,546 and LM microcapsules patterned on solid lines.547 These methods can also be transferred to be used in PSCs. At this point, self-healing in PSCs still is not fully developed. For specific consideration of halide perovskites, some aspects of this material are serious issues for industrial implementation, including the environmental impact from the lead548–550 as well as the materials’ photo- and chemical-stability.551–553 The material is intrinsically metastable with significant anharmonicity in the chemical bonds and consequent dynamic disordered states within the crystallographic lattice.554 Restoring the properties from a ‘damaged’ state of the halide perovskite to its original ‘healthy’ state to prevent device failure is of great importance. As an organic–inorganic hybrid material with a ‘soft’ attribute (a much smaller Young's modulus than those of typical inorganic semiconductors such as Si and GaAs), it is possible to heal the perovskite using the aforementioned methods applied in other ‘soft’ materials such as polymers. For example, using chemical/physical treatments (e.g., exposure to degrading chemical, thermal, or light treatments) on halide perovskites,555,556 cracks and pin-holes can be merged well. Early studies have revealed that the incorporation of polyethylene glycol (PEG) additives into the MAPbI3 perovskite can revert the degraded crystal (yellow δ-phase) back to the black β-phase.557 Specifically as shown in Fig. 25a(i), by infiltrating the perovskite into a PEG scaffold, the composite film exhibits a recovery behavior after degradation upon water spray, which otherwise would display an irreversible degradation if no PEG was involved. By constructing the PEG scaffold-based PSC (Fig. 25a(ii)), a self-healing device performance has also been obtained. The PCE decays with reduced VOC and JSC upon exposure to water vapour but then returns to its original value in less than one minute (Fig. 25a(iii)). This self-healing phenomenon might originate from the excellent hygroscopicity of the PEG coupled by its strong interaction with perovskite lattice terminals. PEG has stronger interactions with water molecules than those of the perovskite and water, so the water can be retained in the PEG phase instead of diffusing into the perovskite phase; PEG can also keep the MAI (through hydrogen interactions) from escaping away through a higher volume of water spray. By putting back into a dry atmosphere, as long as the retained water molecules evaporate, the non-perovskite yellowish phase turned back to the black β-phase. Similar to polymers, small organics with proper interactions with the perovskite lattice can also assist the self-healing of the PSC. Recently, Ran et al. reported the incorporation of large-volume amines (LVAs) in the Sn-based PSC with a self-healing property. After heating or exposing to air (the Sn-based PSC is highly sensitive to heat and oxygen due to the quick oxidation of Sn2+ in FASnI3), the LVA-based device could partially restore its PCE from 60% of its initial value to 80% after being placed in a N2 atmosphere for a specific time. This self-healing effect might be due to the steric hindrance effect of the LVAs.558
 Fig. 25  Self-healing perovskite solar cell against environmental degradation. (a) (i) Photographs of perovskite films with and without PEG showing color change evolution after water-spraying. (ii) Schematic illustration of the PEG scaffold structured perovskite solar cell (PPSC). (iii) J–V curves of the PPSC before and after water spray showing the self-healing feature, adapted with permission from ref. 557, Copyright 2016 Springer. (b) (i) Schematic illustration and cross-sectional SEM image showing an inverted-structure perovskite solar cell. (ii) Normalized solar-cell parameters (VOC, JSC, FF) as a function of the proton dose, ϕ. (iii) Time dependence of the normalized solar-cell parameters after termination of the proton irradiation for a proton-irradiated device (red circles) and for a reference solar cell (black dot), adapted with permission from ref. 564, Copyright 2016 Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA. (c) (i) Schematic illustration of the biasing experiment. (ii) Schematic diagrams of the evolution of the ion distribution within the perovskite layer under solar cell working conditions, adapted wither permission from ref. 567, Copyright 2017 The Royal Society of Chemistry. (d) (i) Schematic illustration of the planar structured perovskite solar cell device. (ii) Time evolution of JSC under constant illumination and after resting the device in the dark. (iii) Schematic diagrams of the proposed photocurrent degradation and self-healing mechanism, adapted with permission from ref. 564, Copyright 2016 Springer. 
Similar ‘self-healing’ effects in PSCs upon exposure and termination of proton radiation have also been observed.559 This might be more important for outer space and space ship mission applications. The proton (p) radiation, together with helium (He) (resulting from galactic cosmic radiation or solar flares, with a particle flux of 103 to 108 cm−2 s−1 depending on the distance from Earth),560–562 is the major cosmic radiation source for the solar cells and MPS used in spatial missions (e.g., spaceships and satellites in the outer space). Protons with high energies (close to 1 MeV) can induce ionization and lattice displacement, and result in device damage to solar cells.563 Lang et al.559 found that the PSC exhibited compromised PCE upon proton irradiation but displayed a self-healing effect by returning the PCE to its original value after terminating the radiation (Fig. 25b(i)). This can be understood from the proton irradiation-induced displacement of hydrogen. The proton irradiation typically results in the chemical bond breakage of C–H and N–H, rendering localized in-band states (further leading to Shockley–Read–Hall (SRH) recombination) (Fig. 25b(ii)). After termination of the proton irradiation, the displaced hydrogen atoms migrate back towards the lattice and passivate the localized defects, recovering to their original state. This self-healing process is verified in the recovery of the photocurrent (Fig. 25b(iii)).
Besides the above moisture and proton radiation stimulated self-healing, another type of healing is via illumination.564 Several groups have reported such photo-induced degradation and recovery of the device PV parameters under constant solar irradiation, applied external bias and storage in the dark. In early 2015, Bag et al.565 reported the first observation of photoinduced quasi-reversibility of the performance of PSCs (MAxFA1−xPbI3). The PSC degradation under sunlight contains both thermally activated fast ion transport and lattice-expansion triggered by IR light in the solar spectrum. Interestingly, they also observed that, after keeping a previously illuminated MAPbI3 PSC in the dark for 15 min, the lost PCE of the device can be recovered when re-illuminated. Similarly, Yadav et al.566 also found that, under conditions of illumination and bias, the JSC of the PSC decreased in a reversible pathway and recovered to its initial number after being kept in the dark for a short time. The ion movement and accumulation, and the resultant interfacial change at selective contacts is concluded to be a possible mechanism restricting the PV performance recovery.566 These ion-migration induced recoverable PCE changes in PSCs have been further supported by the work of Domanski et al.567 (Fig. 25c). By in situ cooling the working PSCs and exemption of thermally induced non-reversible PCE loss, the device exhibits a significant PCE loss under continuous operation (in light) and then shows PCE recovery to its initial value after resting in the dark. As shown in Fig. 25c(ii), in the initial condition, the ionic pairs (stoichiometric anion and cation) are randomly distributed in the perovskite layer; in the non-stabilized condition (devices are exposed to light and biased to MPP) anions (vacancies) migrate to the HTL side and form a Debye layer at the interface, leaving the relatively immobile cation behind; at longer timescales over 103 s (i.e., the stabilized condition), cations migrate and form another Debye layer at the ETL side. Both Debye layers at the HTL and ETL sides are harmful for photocarrier extraction and hence lead to PCE degradation, but it is recoverable. Particularly when the PSC device is given several hours of ‘resting’ in the dark, the ionic distribution can thermodynamically return to its initial random state and thereby the device appears to be ‘fresh’ again and thus the PCE is restored. Supported by modelling and elemental depth profiling results, the slow ionic migration (timescale >103 s) induced reversible PCE losses are further verified. Based on this timescale of >103 s, the natural day/night cycles (timescale of 104 s) can provide enough time for self-healing of the device performance spontaneously. A similar recoverable behaviour of the PSC was also observed by Lee et al.568 After being exposed to 365 nm UV light for >1000 hours under inert gas (<0.5 ppm H2O), followed by one-sun soaking (allowing dispatch of stacked charges and neutralization of defects), the PSC showed PCE recovery. In parallel, a claim of ‘light-activated metastable trap-states’564 to explain the recovery behaviour of PSCs has also been documented. Nie et al.564 demonstrated that PSC degradation under constant solar illumination is due to the photocurrent loss, which can quickly self-heal within 1 min in the dark to its original value (Fig. 25d(ii)). PSC capacitance measurements and film time-resolved (TR) PL spectroscopy attribute the observed photocurrent loss to the ‘light-activated metastable trap-states’564 (Fig. 25(iii)), which further leads to the space charges at the interface (resulting in non-homogeneous electric fields interfering with charge transport in the device) as supported by both experimental data and DFT analysis. Overall, in PSCs there have been several interesting phenomena observed for ‘self-healing’: (i) two distinct time regimes of recovery: fast recovery (i.e., <1 min,564 retrieving ∼96% of its initial PCE), slower recovery (tens of minutes to an hour, i.e., 103 s,567 fully restoring the photocurrent); (ii) the photocurrent degradation can be attributed to the formation of light-activated, metastable and deep trap-states (>0.5 eV);564 (iii) both the degradation and self-healing of PSCs are highly sensitive to temperature and light,565 the former's dynamic (timescale of 103 s) being slower than that of the latter (timescale of 102 s); (iv) a VOC decrease by 40 mV has been observed and is due to the deep trap-states. To explain these behaviours, a more general and fundamental property of the perovskite material needs to be clarified, the ‘polaron’ model.569 The formation of small polarons is generally responsible for this experimental ‘self-healing’ behaviour in PSCs based on the following considerations: (i) localized polaronic states can be manifested as deep trap-states in the forbidden band, which further gives rise to the space charge accumulation and a ≤40% photocurrent reduction; (ii) the formation time of small polarons is faster than the time scales of PSC degradation; (iii) the slow degradation of the photocurrent can be attributed to the accumulation of small polarons. Considering these, Nan et al.570 provided a first-principles calculation on the light-induced photocurrent degradation and self-healing in MAPbI3 PSCs. It is demonstrated that, under photoexcitation, changes of the crystallographic lattice (due to photo-activation of site vacancies, followed by geometric relaxation) lead to the formation of partially localized excitons being trapped at the defects. In the meantime, the other excitons can be dissociated into free charges at the femtosecond scale due to their very small exciton binding energy.571,572 Hence there is the coexistence of trapped excitons and free charges. The trapped excitons can be released (to yield a recovery effect) with an activation energy of 26 meV for the I-site vacancy and 38 meV for the Pb-site vacancy in MAPbI3. Overall, the photocurrent degradation and recovery in PSCs can be determined by the competition between the trapping and release of excitons. This mechanism is more general and can be applied for similar perovskites such as FAPbI3, MAPbBr3, and FAPbBr3, but with a faster recovery dynamic.569 The optical ‘damage’ to the perovskite film has also been investigated and found in the recovery of the PL of multiple perovskite films.569,573 These observations indicate that illumination induced ‘damage’ of the perovskite can be repaired continuously.
Outside the self-healing of the perovskite crystals, another consideration can be the quarantine of leakage of the toxic lead by some self-healing encapsulation materials when the PSC is mechanically damaged. Recently, Jiang et al.574 reported a self-healing polymer-based encapsulation to reduce the lead leakage from damage. Three encapsulation methods (Fig. 26a) have been investigated coupled with realistic simulated mechanical damage (to simulate the real hail impact) as well as Pb leakage rate. The crucial factor to minimize the Pb leakage is the self-repair ability of encapsulating polymers. Fig. 26b and c compare the different encapsulation methods in terms of their effectiveness in limiting the lead leakage. Fig. 26b shows the photographs of PSC modules with different encapsulation methods before impact and Fig. 26c shows the photographs of these PSC modules after mechanical impacts (followed by two water dripping tests, the first by heating at 45 °C and a second water dripping test). Different encapsulation methods yield various degrees of perovskite decomposition. Particularly, an epoxy resin (ER)-based polymer with a Tg of ∼42 °C, when sandwiched between a PSC module and a top capping glass, can effectively reduce the rate of Pb leakage from 30 to 0.08 mg h−1 m−2 under various simulated weather conditions. Taking advantage of such ‘self-healing’ attributes from the polymeric encapsulation materials to protect the device, quarantine the lead, and even recover the partial performance loss is a promising pathway to extend the lifecycle of PSC based MPS products. On a broader scale beyond the epoxy resin, self-healing polymers involving various chemical processes (including incorporation of covalent,575–577 free-radical,578,579 and supramolecular580–582 dynamic bonds) and physical events (such as employing enhanced van der Waals interactions,583 interdigitated copolymeric morphologies – embedded, reactive, encapsulated fluids that can infiltrate into a wound and trigger sequential chemical reactions for damage repair584 and cardiovascular networks585) are providing new insights. Although artificial self-healing in versatile systems may contain complex mechanisms, self-healing in living systems involves complex cascade reaction series, the exact chemistries of some of which are far from being understood. Resembling these natural self-healing processes from learning and mimicking the reactions at the molecular scale is the major ongoing task in the community of artificial self-healing materials. Learning, transferring, and applying these techniques into the more specialized PSC field may create a big leap to self-healable MPS products.
 Fig. 26  Self-quarantine strategies in PSCs. (a) Schematic illustration showing the different encapsulation methods (left) and the experimental procedure to assess the quantity of the toxic Pb leaked from a perovskite solar module that is damaged due to an external impact (for example, hail) (right). (b) and (c) Perovskite solar modules using different encapsulation methods (b) before and (c) after impact and water dripping test, adapted with permission from ref. 574, Copyright 2019 Springer. 

5.4 Bio-additives
Incorporation of processing additives into the photoactive layer is a universally effective methodology to boost PV cell performance. In OPVs, small molecule additives can tune the micromorphology of the BHJ blend and hence tune multiple electronic properties;288 in PSCs, additives have been reported to have multiple effects on the device performance. There are various types of additives including salts, molecules, polymers, and even nanoparticles,46,586–588 with varied effects, including modulating crystalline features, stabilizing the perovskite phase, adjusting energy-level alignment, enhancing operational stability, eliminating hysteresis, suppressing nonradiative recombination, etc.589,590 In contrast to those inorganic additives, biomolecular additives may have additional functionalities through molecular interactions with the perovskite lattice by their specific functional chemical groups. A good example is in caffeine,591 which is the active substance in coffee. A recent study has revealed the effectiveness of incorporation of caffeine in boosting the stability of PSCs. The caffeine molecule has two CO groups (Fig. 27a(i)) with lone pair electrons that could interact with the Pb2+ from the perovskite lattice through strong Coulombic coordination, serving as a ‘molecular interlock’ between adjacent perovskite grains. Meanwhile, these molecules can serve as Lewis bases passivating the Lewis acid-type defects like undercoordinated Pb2+ in perovskites, thus reducing the trap density. This molecular interlock effect also suppresses the ion migration, yielding a superior stability for a PSC based on the n–i–p structure (Fig. 27a(ii)) to retain 90% of the initial PCE after 1700 hours when annealed at 85 °C (in contrast to 60% of the initial performance remaining from the reference blank device after 175 hours of annealing, as shown in Fig. 27a(iii)). Similarly, Zhang et al.592 introduced N719 (a dye derived from chlorophyll) into the MAPbI3 perovskite crystal. The –COOH groups from N719 were found to form complexes with the MA+ (MA+ = CH3NH3+) in the perovskite lattice (Fig. 27b(i)), which modifies the diffusion of water molecules in the materials. Theoretical calculations show a much higher migration barrier of water molecules in the N719-based perovskite. As a result, the water penetration into the perovskite layer can be greatly inhibited (Fig. 27b(ii)), and the moisture stability of the device is significantly improved. Nearly 70% of the PCE can be retained at a high RH of 60% for 400 hours (Fig. 27b(iii)). Our group also revealed that the incorporation of hydrophobic DNA–CTMA into the perovskite crystal can sufficiently improve the device stability282 by nanoscopically wrapping the perovskite nanocrystals (Fig. 27c(i)). The strong hydrophobicity of DNA–CTMA also enhances the non-wettability upon contact with water (Fig. 27c(ii)) and thereby improves the device lifetime (Fig. 27c(iii)). Recently, our group reported a ‘perovskite/perovskite’ bilayer structure. The bottom perovskite layer is a bulk layer with a typical grain size of hundreds of nanometers, while the top perovskite layer consists of perovskite–artemisinin core–shell nanocrystals. This bilayer structure is spontaneously formed by adding the artemisinin molecules (Fig. 27d(i)), which have been known as medicine molecules against malaria.581,593 Artemisinin has a unique endoperoxide ring that produces free radicals to damage the proteins of parasites and thereby cure the disease.594,595 The sesquiterpene lactone structure and alkyls make artemisinin insoluble in water, showing a hydrophobic behavior.591 As a result, the hydrophobicity from the artemisinin-incorporated MAPbI3 top layer (Fig. 27d(ii)) improves the device stability with a retained efficiency >18% which corresponds to ∼95% of the initial PCE after one-month exposure to ambient environment (Fig. 27d(iii)). Overall, by providing functional groups to anchor the terminal partially bonded atoms in the perovskite lattice, the biomolecular additives are effective in improving the stability of the crystal. In addition, the PCEs of the resultant PSCs can be improved to certain degrees mostly due to the enhanced crystalline quality. Fig. 28 summarizes the PCEs of the PSCs incorporated with multiple bio-molecular additives. The highest PCE of 20.63% comes from the device structure of FTO/TiO2/perovskite/spiro-MeOTAD/Au with the addition of DNA–CTMA into the perovskite layer. Nevertheless, biomolecules are naturally more complex than typically used artificial additives and their role in the cascade reaction series in a species can be even more complicated and delicate. Harnessing their specific chemical group as a functional agent to couple with those active terminal atoms/crystallographic sites in the perovskite lattice might be a good way to improve performance, but it remains technically inefficient and scientifically unclear when using such complex molecules to execute simple redox chemical reactions between biomolecules and the perovskite lattice. Instead, simplifying the biomolecule structures, designing an optimal structure using the knowledgebase and knowhow from aforementioned successes, and utilizing a more functional-oriented bio-additives for PSCs might be a better alternative.
 Fig. 27  Bio-additives in perovskite solar cells. (a) (i) Molecular structure of caffeine. (ii) Cross-sectional SEM image of the perovskite solar cell. (iii) Thermal stability test under 85 °C continuous annealing and photovoltaic performance of the caffeine-incorporated solar cell, adapted with permission from ref. 591, Copyright 2019 Elsevier. (b) (i) Schematic illustration showing the interaction between MA+ and N-719. (ii) Photograph showing the degradation transformation process of the perovskite film with and without the N-719 additive. (iii) Stability test of the perovskite solar cell at a 60% relative humidity, adapted with permission from ref. 592, Copyright 2019 Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. (c) (i) Schematic illustration showing the interaction between DNA and the perovskite. (ii) Contact angle of a water droplet on the pristine and DNA-modified perovskites. (iii) Stability test of the perovskite solar cell with and without DNA modification at a relative humidity of 75%, adapted with permission from ref. 282, Copyright 2019 American Chemical Society. (d) (i) Molecular structure of artemisinin (ART). (ii) Schematic and cross-sectional SEM image showing the bilayer-structured perovskite solar cell induced by ART. (iii) Stability test of pristine and ART-modified perovskite solar cells at a relative humidity of 50%, adapted with permission from ref. 481, Copyright 2020 Elsevier. 
 Fig. 28  Summary of bio-additives in different classes of photovoltaic devices: (1&2) Hou et al. ref. 282, (3) Wang et al., ref. 591, (4) Das et al., ref. 328, (5) Yusoff et al., ref. 281, (6) Deng et al., ref. 596, (7) Dajar et al., ref. 279, (8) Nie et al., ref. 597, (9) Toschi et al., ref. 597, (10 & 11) Wang et al., ref. 404, (12) Henning et al., ref. 598, (13) Calogero et al., ref. 402, and (14) Kumara et al., ref. 599. 
5.5 Bio-hybrids
Extending from the scope of the abovementioned nanoscale or molecular-scale mixing of biomolecules and semiconductors, blending organisms with semiconductors at the meso-scale is another research interest. Classic examples such as dye-sensitized solar cells where natural dyes are adsorbed at the surface of TiO2 to execute the light-triggered biological processes across their interface, or photo-bioelectrochemical cells and photobiofuel cells with biomolecules, cells or bacteria immobilized on the metal electrodes have been extensively studied for applications in energy harvesting, sensing and storage.600–602 Particularly, similar to the photoactive layer of perovskite in a perovskite solar cell, the photosynthetic proteins (e.g., Reaction Centre – Light Harvesting 1 (RC-LH1)) from natural organisms such as Rhodobacter sphaeroides603 play the key role in natural photosynthesis reactions because of not only their similar role in photoexcitation of electrons to that of the semiconductor but also their unique attributes such as ultrahigh quantum efficiency for energy transfer and charge separation as well as the maintenance of this high efficiency at low temperatures such as 4 K.604,605 Recently, Tan et al.604 took advantage of the wide response window of photosynthetic proteins to light, temperature, pressure, etc., and developed self-powered all-weather sensory systems. By incorporating RC-LH1 into an electrolyte medium of a triblock copolymer (thermoreversible gel (TRG)), the photoelectrochemical cell even exhibited a self-healing behavior. This successful demonstration of photosynthetic proteins in solar-driven sensor applications revealed the underlying potential of natural photosynthetic proteins in innovating multifunctional solar devices.
Similarly, bacteriorhodopsin (bR) is another well-known photoactive protein usually found in the purple membrane of Halobacterium salinarum. In Section 4.3, we have discussed their role in the charge transport layer in solar cells. Beyond charge transport, in light of the enhanced potential for light-induced proton pumping and color switching, bR has also been adopted as a platform for various applications such as fuel cells, photodetectors, biosensors, artificial retinas, nanosensors, storage devices, holographic memory, security inks, and bio-defense or camouflage, which have been well documented in another review paper.606
Although not well studied so far, interfacing these relatively larger-size proteins with perovskite photoactive layer materials to render new composites could potentially extend the application domains of solar cells. For example, the low-temperature environment workable RC-LH1 could extend the solar cells’ application range to meet a critical environmental condition of extremely cold weather. Technically, employing these larger-size proteins in vitro in a perovskite polycrystalline layer still needs to be properly strategized, where prior learnings from effective immobilization of proteins on the electrode of a photoelectrochemical device may be helpful. In parallel, fundamental research on the natural function of inter- and intra-proteins and full understanding of the efficient transfer of photoinduced electrons across the interface are also necessary and important to guide us as to how to mimic it in real devices.
6. From the device to system level
In the above text, we have discussed the bio-inspired strategy for each individual component in the PSC device, including the electrode, the charge transfer layer and the perovskite photoactive layer. Zooming out from these individual components towards the perspective at the device or system level, more intriguing and creative designs of the whole device system and their corresponding manufacturing inspired by the natural bio-systems are of great interest. In the following section, bio-inspired design with successful reported demonstrations at the whole device level and unconventional manufacturing techniques replicating the sophisticated nano/microstructures from biology will be discussed.
6.1 Bio-inspired design

6.1.1 Solar tracker. 
Many living creatures can track light adaptively to maximize their exposure to the light. This light-tracking phenomenon is known as phototropism, which can be found in the example of a sunflower that can self-orient itself to face the sun throughout the day. In this tropic movement, organisms not only spatially ‘sense’ the light stimuli, but also spontaneously and continuously adjust their movements to strictly follow the incident light directions. This presents intelligence in self-regulation through the natural feedback control inherent in dynamic interactions between the stimulus and the organisms. As the natural sunlight is dynamic (Earth rotation), there is an inevitable oblique-incidence energy-density loss (OEL, sunlight density reduction when the emissive energy projects on a surface obliquely, as shown in Fig. 29a). The loss could be enormous if the incident angle is large (e.g., 75% loss at 75° incidence). In order to maintain the high-level light-reception, many plants developed such phototropism behavior. This phototropism could provide an efficient solution to address the universal OEL issue and huge energy loss in existing solar panel farms, although solar trackers have been invented to reduce the OEL but mostly need additional power to supply their rotation (will be discussed later). Harnessing such phototrophic behavior autonomously, without external control or power supply, is of great significance in achieving highly efficient and intelligent PV systematic platforms with smart autonomous adaption to complex ambient environments (such as clouds and sunset). Although many reversibly photoresponsive soft materials such as hydrogels,611–616 liquid crystal elastomers617–619 and azobenzene/spiropyran-based polymers620,621 have been proposed as good candidates for achieving this artificial phototropism behavior,607 their applications in solar trackers of PVs remain fully unexplored.
 Fig. 29  Solar tracking. (a) Schematic illustration showing concept of the artificial phototropism and the sunflower-like biomimetic omnidirectional tracker, adapted with permission from ref. 607, Copyright 2019 Springer. (b) Schematic illustration of the solar hour angle which is controlled by the daily rotation of the Earth, adapted from an online resource.608 (c) Photograph of a sunflower (left), adapted from an online resource,609 and a sunflower-shaped solar panel (right), adapted from an online resource.610 
In the case of PVs, the output electrical power is heavily dependent on the solar irradiation that reaches the PV cell surface.622,623 The more orthogonal the sunlight reaching the cell surface, the higher the output power that can be achieved. The Earth is constantly spinning and the incident angle of the Sun's rays on the cell surface is dynamically changing, causing significant power loss of the PV cell. In addition to the Earth's daily rotation, the annual rotation of the tilted Earth (Fig. 29b), the latitude of the surface, and the local weather conditions will also affect the light absorption of the PV cell. In order to expand the overall solar harvesting, the solar tracking technology inspired by phototropism is a good solution. The state-of-the-art solar tracking techniques (Fig. 29c) include the passive tracker (e.g., liquid- or gas-based thermal expansion mechanism by the company Zomewords,624 and researchers like Clifford et al.625 and Castañeda et al.626) and the active tracker (e.g., control logic and algorithm based mechanism, first presented by McFee,627 a control system with a microprocessor and an electro-optical sensor628–633). The former is advantageous as it has less energy/cost consumption of the tracking system, but it lacks intelligent reaction to the weather (as it is mostly thermally driven, hot weather will significantly interfere with the tracking motion). The latter is advantageous in dynamically adapting the complex environmental conditions, but requires additional power supply to maintain its response to environmental stimuli, an algorithm to respond to the temporal weather change and responding motion.
The ultimate way of harvesting solar energy is to construct a sphere surrounding the light source. In a more cosmoscopic level, efficiently utilizing the solar energy in the Solar System by future PV technology beyond the ongoing state-of-the-art technology might be more exciting although unrealistic. For example, it could be a megastructure or the so-called ‘Dyson sphere’634 conceived of as a gigantic shell of solar cells enclosing the sun and harvest 400 septillion watts of energy per second (a trillion times our current worldwide energy usage).


6.1.2 Scaffolds for individual micro-cells. 
In real applications, the failure tolerance (the property that enables a system to continue operating properly in the event of the failure of (or one or more faults within) some of its components) is an important index for the product. Particularly for PSCs, the perovskite is highly sensitive to the ambient environment, making it easily degrade during normal outdoor operation. When one ‘strip’ cell in the PV module fails (e.g., may be due to small mechanical fracture, a small hole or even a microscale contact issue), the whole module may not work. In nature, such an issue can be avoided through design of features like an insect's compound eye (Fig. 30a). The compound eyes are composed of many thousands (30 thousands in larger Anisoptera dragonflies) of facets (or ommatidium, hexagonal in cross-section and a large aspect ratio with ten times longer than wide) (Fig. 30a(i)). Each ommatidium works as an individual photon sensor (as there is a cluster of photoreceptor cells at the inner wall of an ommatidium which can convert the light signal into a nerve pulse for reception by optical nerves). Hence, each ommatidium is innervated by one axon and thus providing the brain with one picture element. The brain forms an image from these independent picture elements. Such a design enables the independent work of an ommatidium with exemption of the interference from a neighboring ommatidium. In other words, the failure of an ommatidium will not affect the normal function of neighboring ommatidia. Using this concept, a ‘compound eye’ PSC has been developed recently,435 which contains individual cells being partitioned by a scaffold and arranged into a vast array of microcells with characteristics including a smaller size, encapsulation, and mechanical and chemical isolation. Fig. 30b shows the design. The (gray) partitioning scaffold shields the individual perovskite microcells. The microcells (in the array) are connected in parallel by both the top and bottom electrodes. The array has a pitch of 500 μm, which can be further modulated by adjusting the design of the scaffold substrates. As a result, thanks to the mechanical stress de-concentration, this ‘compound’ PSC displays a significantly enhanced fracture energy of ∼13 J m−2 (30-fold increase over a prior reported planar PSC with a value of ∼0.4 J m−2) with over 15% PCE. This design could also improve the failure tolerance; since all the microcells are electrically connected in parallel, failure of any device is expected to not cause the failure of the whole device.
 Fig. 30  Scaffold for individual micro-cell. (a) (i) Structure of a compound eye, adapted from an online resource ref. 637. (ii) Photograph of the compound eye of a fly, adapted with permission from ref. 435. (iii) Image of an object formed in the compound eye, adapted from an online resource ref. 638. (b) Schematic illustration of the “compound” perovskite solar cell design inspired by the compound eye concept, adapted with permission from ref. 435, Copyright 2017 The Royal Society of Chemistry. (c) (i) Schematic illustration of the human eye structure. (ii) Confocal microscope image of rod and cone photoreceptors in a human retina, adapted from an online resource ref. 639. (iii) Illustration of the structure of cone and rod photoreceptors in the human retina, adapted with permission from ref. 640, Copyright 2016 Elsevier. (d) Schematic illustration and microstructure of the artificial retina based on the perovskite nanowire, which is mounted on a concavely hemispherical artificial eye, adapted with permission from ref. 636, Copyright 2020 Springer. 
This individual array design for the vision system prevails in nature, although the detailed mechanism in photon-sensing can be versatile. An analogous example is the retina's neurons that can recognize the light and convert it into neural signals. The retina is composed of three different layers: (i) outer nuclear layer (ONL, where the well-known cone and rod cells can sense the light and convert it into neural signals), (ii) inner nuclear layer (INL, where the horizontal, bipolar, and amacrine cells can transmit the neural signals from the ONL to the GCL), and (iii) ganglion cell layer (GCL, where the retinal ganglion cells can receive the neural signals from the INL and further transmit the action ionic potentials to the visual cortex through optic nerves) (Fig. 30c(i)).635Fig. 30c(ii) shows the picture of real cone cells (typically 40–50 μm long and 0.5 to 4.0 μm wide) and rod cells (typically 100 μm long and 2 μm wide). The most important part composing the rod and cone cells are the numerous membrane disks (Fig. 30c(iii)) containing large numbers of photoreceptor proteins, rhodopsin. It is the rhodopsin that acts as the visual pigment to convert the light into biochemical signals. As the rhodopsin photoreceptor protein is inserted in the membrane, the disk-like packing of the membrane can enhance the population of photoreceptor proteins and improve the light sensitivity. Such an individually arrayed rod and cone layout perpendicularly inserted at the retina layer enables an efficient integration of a large quantity of individual cells, leading to a high visual resolution. Designing a retina-like photoactive array layer for PSCs on a hemisphere substrate (Fig. 30d) to panoramically harvest light might be an alternative design to the dynamic solar tracker-system. One recent study on artificial retina conceptualized the perovskite nanorod (NR) array on a concavely hemispherical artificial eye (field of view (FOV) of 150°–160°).636 A hemispherical porous aluminum oxide membrane (PAM) was used as the template with numerous microchannels where the perovskite single crystalline NR can grow (Fig. 30d).
Overall, many examples exist with a large potential for the use of nature-inspired designs into novel structured PSCs. Shifting our attention from the focus on individual components such as each functional layer or material towards the whole device level design, system level implementation, temporal life-cycle and recycling, network overall planning with those power consumers and other power plants could bring new insights to upgrade the device to the next level, which definitely will still need more knowledge and intelligence. These knowledgebases may have already been stored in the library of nature.

6.2 Bio-inspired manufacturing
Natural micro/nanostructures (including the above-discussed nacre, bone, Lotus leaves, insect eyes and wings, etc.) have provided enormous inspiration to many important applications. In addition to those research efforts devoted to further understanding the underlying mechanisms, there is a growing effort to manufacture artificial materials with those unprecedented properties by mimicking those found in nature. Focusing on superhydrophobicity and superhydrophilicity in nature as examples, Jiang et al. proposed the general procedure of designing and synthesizing related artificial materials:641 (i) unique property selection from the biological system (bio-target), (ii) an in-depth understanding of the correlation between the natural structure and their macroscopic properties, (iii) selection of the target artificial material, and (iv) construction of the architecture to realize the aimed function. Nevertheless, the major challenge lies in techniques chosen to assemble the molecules at both nano- and microscales. So far, various strategies to synthesize functional materials that resemble natural species’ architecture and function have been developed, including chemical vapor deposition (CVD),642–645 self-assembly,646–651 lithography,652–658 and other 3D printing techniques.659 Particularly, additive manufacturing (3D printing, exemplified in Fig. 31a) has opened new opportunities for mimicking and manufacturing the intrinsic multi-scale, multi-material, and multi-functional structures found in nature.660 Nevertheless, in contrast to most man-made materials, natural materials are built in much milder inner environments within the species’ body which has ambient temperatures and an aqueous environment. The ‘manufacturing’ of natural materials is more delicate and involves bottom-up strategies in a tiny system, making them difficult to duplicate in large-scale artificial manufacturing.661 Importantly, natural materials combining the desirable properties of their components usually perform significantly better than the sum of their parts (similar to the emergent properties)—a natural coupling effect that has inspired much of the ongoing interest in the design of bioinspired materials. Many natural systems simply follow basic mathematical rules but turned out to be sophisticated functionalities (e.g., ant's route selection of emergency logistics algorithm).662 Exploring and learning the secrets from natural materials and systems offer a pathway towards solving manufacturing challenges that exist in the current PSC-based MPS. By using bio-inspiration, innovations may exist either in the individual manufacturing of the PSCs or in their integration with the MPS in a higher-level network incorporating both energy supplier and energy consumer for advanced internet of energy (IoE) systems. Stream-line manufacturing with a continuous producing attribute could allow the PSC fabrication in a highly efficient way, which will need to balance the conflicts between each individual component in the device and eventually unite them into a whole device with consistent functionality. Since the PSC consists of multiple components including the bottom electrode (front contact), ETL, perovskite light absorber, HTL, and top electrode (back contact) as well as interfacial modification layers sometimes, each component will need a specific individual manufacturing process to obtain its optimal performance, and this diversification in manufacturing processes will make it costly and time-consuming for the whole device fabrication. In comparison, streamlined manufacturing in conjunction with a joint consideration on both cost and performance of the whole device will need to minimize the conflict behaviors and maximize the reciprocal behaviors between each individual component. A natural prototype of this strategy could be the human gut microbial ecosystem (HGME) that consists of hundreds to thousands of species of bacteria, archaea, and eukarya as well as viruses.663 In order to maintain a eubiotic state that promotes homeostasis of the whole bodily function, individual species in the HGME (individual component in the system) could sacrifice in a certain level to reduce conflicts and enhance the reciprocals to keep this mutualism as well as their own sustainability. Regulating the individual components with the aim of achieving the optimization of the whole system is thus an important scenario in nature species. Smart regulation and intelligent and timely communication between components could thus lead to a more efficient resource/information/energy usage, which has been recognized as the internet of things (IoT) technology. Similarly, distributed energy systems could also optimize the efficiency of energy infrastructure and reduce the waste (Fig. 31b). Focusing on energy efficiency, this system-level design mainly includes the insertion, implementation, installation, and coordination of the MPS in a higher-level system. For example, MPS can be used as the power plant for a ‘MotorHome’, supplying powers to all the appliances used in the recreational vehicle (RV), where an intelligent mini-IoE system can more efficiently manage the supply–consumption correlation between the MPS and the consumer streams. This MPS-incorporated smart IoE concept can be further used in those ‘smart home’ and ‘smart city’ projects, where multiple types of MPS such as solar windows, solar tiles, solar roofs and solar farms can be incorporated as a node in the energy cyber network analogous to nature's “ant's nest” where the delivery of electricity follows a highly efficient traffic way that is free from any jam or shortage.
 Fig. 31  Bio-inspired manufacturing and system level management. (a) (i) Schematic illustration of the electrically assisted 3D-printing equipment. (ii) Illustration of the bottom-up projection-based stereolithography process, adapted with permission from ref. 664, Copyright 2019 AAAS. (b) Illustration of the concept internet of energy (IoE). 
7. Conclusion
High-performance natural materials and systems have been well developed over millions of years’ evolution and present valuable sources for inspiration in engineering both (i) the individual material components in a subsystem and (ii) the whole integrated system. PSCs incorporating the biomimicry by learning from nature's concepts and designs are expected to be a new route towards next-generation PV-MPS technologies like their prior success in driving a paradigm shift in the development of other modern science and technology communities. In terms of individual material components in a PSC subsystem, there have been successes in demonstrating highly efficient bio-inspired strategies to improve the performance and properties of electrodes (e.g., ‘biomimetic ant-nest electrode structure’, ‘leaf vein electrode network’, other nature's fractural design inspired hierarchy electrode), the CTL (e.g., DNA, fractal neuron network, bR-inspired composite charge transfer materials, and other natural molecules such as porphyrins, natural conjugates and carbonized material incorporated CTL), and the perovskite photoactive layer (e.g., specific micro-composite inspired by nacre, bamboo, and wood, functional microscopic design aiming at self-cleaning and self-healing inspired by many natural examples, and many bio-molecular additives to improve the specific property of the perovskite crystal) of the PSC. In the case of system and overall device design, potential bio-inspiration for MPS applications includes solar trackers (inspired by sunflower and phototropism of plants), micro-cell arrays composed of sub-cells separated by a scaffold (inspired by insect compound eye arrays), and various bio-related manufacturing that have been or are being developed (although perfectly mimicking the manufacturing of natural species remains an open question in the whole field of biomimicry, 3D printing and other state-of-the-art techniques are helping to achieve a closer step).
7.1 Prospects
In the specific case of PSC-based MPS, the future bio-inspiration may focus on the downsides of current PSCs. Compared with incumbent PV techniques such as Si, CIGS, CdTe PVs and OPVs, PSCs are more advantageous in terms of the relatively high PCE (due to the superior optoelectronic properties of halide perovskite materials), low cost (solution-processability of the core perovskite photoactive layer and other charge transfer layers), high degree of flexibility and large power density (lightweight device for a high output power due to the large light extinction coefficient of the perovskite so that a thin layer can harvest a high intensity of light), which make them potential candidates for the next-generation PV-MPS. Nevertheless, the drawbacks of PSCs include the (i) mechanical fragility and brittleness, (ii) environmental impact, (iii) life-time and life-cycle issue, and (iv) limitations in scale-up manufacturing, which might be the specific focus where the bio-inspiration can pop in. In addition to these, (v) the system-level design to intelligently coordinate MPS with other electricity consumers could be another dimension to consider the bio-inspiration. Fig. 32a shows the ‘Cannikin Law’ (Wooden Bucket Theory) of PV-MPS, where the bio-inspiration can serve to fill the gap from the shortest staves (five drawbacks of PSCs mentioned above). In particular, for each issue, potential solutions to the shortage of current PSC-based MPS inspired by nature are shown as follows:
 Fig. 32  Challenge and promise. (a) Schematic showing the drawbacks of PSCs hindering its application as MPS. (b) Schematic showing the adoption of a bio-inspired strategy to realize perovskite solar cell MPS. 
(i) Mechanical robustness: at the material level, for each component in a PSC, the trade-off between mechanical and optical (electrical) properties remains a challenge, particularly for the MPS aimed at portable, foldable and stretchable applications. For example, the front transparent electrode of a PSC for wearable MPS requires certain levels of flexibility with qualified optical transmittance and electrical conductivity, while most commonly used flexible TCO-based transparent electrodes (e.g., ITO) coated on plastic substrates (e.g., PET and PEN) usually exhibit electrical conductance failure after long-term mechanical bending. Designing a flexible metal NW network inspired by nature's fractal hierarchy structure (e.g., leaf veins, neural networks) could help boost the charge transport efficiency while maintaining the mechanical endurance; employing the nacre laminar composite structure could further assist in enhancing the mechanical strength. In the case of the CTL, design of an insect's compound eye and/or butterfly wing inspired periodic nanostructure could uniformly disperse the stress to avoid the concentrated stress induced fracture. Moreover, engineering the micromorphology of the perovskite layer by introducing either ordered micro-composite structures inspired by nature's micro-patterns found in various plants or molecular dyes that can simultaneously boost the mechanical strength and electronic properties (through molecular bonding) is of great promise. At the device level, designing and innovating the device architecture inspired by the hemispheric retina to not only redistribute external stress but also enlarge the light harvesting could be another pathway. Such hemispheric design with the PSC sticking outside can further be utilized in those curved roofs, UAV wings, etc. Overall, there have been various strategies in enhancing the mechanical properties of PSCs through versatile bio-inspired designs and materials. As the learning and investigation of nature's secret goes by, there will be growing ingenious strategies inspired and applied for PSC based MPS.
(ii) Environmental impact: the major safety concern of PSCs is the heavy metal pollution of the B-site atom (Pb, Sn, etc.) in the halide perovskite material. Mostly Pb poisoning will increase oxidative stress and consequently cause various deleterious effects on hematopoietic, reproductive, renal, and central nervous systems.665 The US Centers for Disease Control (CDC)666,667 has set the upper limit of the blood lead level at 10 μg dL−1 (10 μg/100 g) and at 5 μg dL−1 for adults and children, respectively. Additionally, elevated Pb levels in the environment can result in decreased growth and reproductive rates of plants and animals, as well as neurological effects in vertebrates. Hence, there is an urgent need to prevent Pb leakage and contamination. So far, the best practical policy is to screen the Pb within the PSCs to prevent the leakage, which however is a heavily under-researched field that needs attention. Some ‘self-healing’ strategies have been introduced recently to sufficiently quarantine the Pb from a broken device.574 The encapsulation of the perovskite within protective shells or alternatively adding reactive agents to bind and quarantine Pb to avoid leakage can also be a possible solution.668 Pharmacologically, the removal of lead is usually performed using ‘chelation therapy’669 (a medical procedure involving the administration of chelating agents to react with and remove heavy metals from the body). For the specific case of Pb, ethylenediaminetetraacetic acid (EDTA) is used as the agent which can bind and hold onto (chelate) Pb. Using such chelating agents in PSCs may also help to ‘hold’ the Pb in the case of PSCs broken under rain (otherwise rain will wash the leaked lead into the environment). In nature, Pb also binds to a variety of different biomolecules ranging from enzymes and proteins through interactions of Pb in thiolate-rich sites found in metalloregulatory proteins (e.g., Pbr, Znt, and CadC) and in enzymes (e.g., δ-aminolevulinic acid dehydratase (ALAD)).670 Incorporating certain biomolecules into PSCs may further anchor the Pb within the device in the case of device breakage and bad weather that would otherwise accelerate the Pb leakage. In addition to these, as water is the major agent that can accelerate the escape of Pb from the PSC, sufficiently preventing the water from infiltrating the device can be effective to prevent the Pb leakage. Following this, some self-cleaning strategies resembling lotus leaves (non-wettability) can help.
(iii) Life-time and life-cycle issue: life-time, life-cycle analysis, investment, pay-back time, levelized cost of energy (LCOE), etc. are important indices to establish a benchmark for pre-industrial and laboratory-scale processes as well as individual MPS applications. As the life-time of PSCs is notoriously shorter (ranging from the month to the year scale, state-of-the-art lifetime ∼10−1 to 100 years) than that of their commercial counterparts (>101 years), the first priority of the PSC industry should be the stability issue. In the case of real applications, critical weather (rain, hail, and snow), hot temperatures, day–night cycling induced light intensity change, and other environmental activities will further establish the baseline requirement of the device lifetime. On the other hand, recycling of the used device at the end of the life-cycle is also important but lacks sufficient research efforts. These fields require highly efficient and intelligent strategies to push the PSC based MPS closer towards the market. Prior bio-inspiration has resulted in early attempts at enhancing the stability of PSCs (e.g., bio-molecular doping to anchor the crystal lattice, introducing hydrophobic bio-molecules in each layer of the device and designing a lotus-like surface structure to repel the water), adaptation to the light change, and other functionalities to adapt the dynamic changes in the environment for elongating the practical lifetime (e.g., self-healing, self-cleaning). More attention may be needed at the life-cycle end. In fact, the arrival of the end-of-lifecycle of a PV is unavoidable in any case. International Renewable Energy Agency (IRENA) estimates that the recyclable materials in retired solar modules will be worth $15 billion in recoverable assets by the year 2050.671 At that specific end point, how to deal with the retired perovskite PVs should be an important topic. The conventional way to deal with PV waste is a disposal process, either by landfill or by incineration. However, both of these disposal methods need capital investment, energy/labor consumption and even consideration of the environmental impact (lead leakage). Alternatively, regenerating the retired PVs by recycling the raw materials and re-collecting them from the PV waste could circumvent these issues. From this scenario, development of a simple but efficient method to regenerate perovskite PVs and extend their lifecycle is of importance.
(iv) Limitations in scale-up manufacturing: the efficient manufacturing capable of continuously producing PSC-based MPS is important. As perovskite-based MPS can be developed for numerous portable uses, their form or design cannot be limited to certain types. For this, suitable manufacturing techniques are required to properly upscale the lab-level PSC device toward MPS-oriented production. For example, MPS products with a ‘wallpaper-like’ feature that can be attached and detached anywhere anytime could be of great interest. Realization of such MPS will need a mature up-scaling manufacturing that can print a large-area PSC device on flexible substrates. Bio-inspired additive manufacturing (e.g., 3D printing bio-photovoltaics) might be a good starting point. While considering the (a) materials’ uniqueness (crystal, instability in ambient environment, critical crystallization requirement), (b) device's complexity (multiple nanometer-scale layer-by-layer structure), (c) high optical and electrical requirements (transmittance, electrical conductance, contact between each layer), and (d) tradeoff between cost and payback, PSC manufacturing will need more delicate design and intensive efforts. Ingeniously designing and innovating a PSC-customized processing by incorporating newly-conceptualized bio-inspired strategies will be a big leap. In parallel, the energy consumption in PSC manufacturing is an important factor affecting its potential in the market. Instead of energy-intensive and cumbersome processing, the biological system can synthesize high property materials (robust mechanical properties in woods and bamboo, magnetic single crystals in jelly fish) through multiple biochemical reactions (e.g., biomineralization672) in a room-temperature, aqueous environment (in contrast, TiO2 in PSCs requires high temperature sintering and perovskite crystallization needs over 100 °C annealing). Simplifying those bio-synthetic methods into an artificial synthetic paradigm that can have a bunch of advantages in the application of PSC manufacturing would be intriguing.
(v) System-level design: by bringing the bio-inspired strategies into the nano-/micro-scale design and the manufacturing techniques, the functionality of PSCs can be strengthened. For example, a solar tracking technology inspired by sunflower has been introduced to solar cell technologies. By dynamically tracking the direction of sunlight, the loss of electricity generation of PSCs from the tilted incident light will be complemented. The angle of incident light is influenced by the various spatial states of Earth (the daily and the annual rotation) and the local environment of the system. The state-of-the-art solar tracking technology should be able to dynamically react to the change of direction of sunlight in energy-efficient and cost-effective ways. Additionally, a scaffold to separate a single device into multiple devices can be a solution. By adopting the scaffold design, inspired by ommatidia (units of the compound eyes of arthropods like insects, crustaceans and millipedes), and rhodopsin of retina, for PSCs, their stability against various stimuli from temperature changes and humidity can be improved. Since each partitioned device can generate electricity individually, the whole device failure can be avoided if the system is partially broken or damaged by external factors. Such a spatial design of the scaffold was inspired by nature to have an efficient arrangement of sub-cells so that each component device can be densely positioned inside. The scaffold also allows the system to have mechanical resilience against fracture, which is enabled by the stress-deconcentrating feature of the scaffold. Synchronization and coordination at the system level can also be found at manufacturing and synthesis sides. A natural ‘organ factory’ could efficiently synthesize multi-functional structures and/or materials. The mimicry of the natural synthetic ways to manufacture artificial materials and a system production of certain functional proteins can bring new opportunities to boost the manufacturing of both perovskite materials and the PSC devices.
7.2 Challenges
Bio-inspiration for next-generation MPS is a multidisciplinary topic that requires expertise related to biology, materials science, engineering, electrics, chemistry and physics. This will require researchers from different backgrounds to team up into a more comprehensive group to have a higher-level overview of the whole field and then come up with targeted strategies to fill up the ‘shortest staves’ of the ‘Wooden Bucket’ in Fig. 32a. Bio-inspiration can provide the shortcut solution to those targeted strategies but remains to be a more general and abstractive concept. Most prior built bio-inspirations are focusing on mechanical and structural engineering, as provided in several well-documented review papers.423,424,660,673–675 For the specific application in PSC, PV-MPS remains not fully explored. This will require more efforts to interface between the parallelly developing PSC and bio-inspiration, as conceptualized in Fig. 32b. Nevertheless, several challenges remain to be addressed:
(i) The synthesis mechanisms found in nature need further investigation and the transformation of the bio-manufacturing to the artificial needs more studies. For application purposes, bio-inspired manufacturing needs to be developed to reveal the mechanisms underlying the intriguing properties. For example, nature grows organisms in a mild environment but takes a long time (bones need years to grow), while artificial manufacturing of PSCs requires a high time efficiency from the minute to the hour scale. Understanding nature's mechanism of material formation (growth) and identifying the origin of the long-time necessity may inspire the creation of a substitutive way to mimic the natural process in a much shorter time. For example, the crystal growth in natural mild conditions (e.g., biomineralization, biological investigation on produced materials, such as shells, teeth and bone, as well as the processes that lead to the formation of these hierarchically structured organic–inorganic composites) may provide implications of designing new synthetic routes. The chemical reaction route and new mechanism found in those bio-prototypes could provide opportunities for growing perovskites in a milder ambient environment instead of the current critical requirements of high temperature and low moisture. This, however, will require an improved understanding and knowledgebase of nature's synthesis.
(ii) Materials in nature are structured and usually a mixture of various kinds of sub-components (e.g., polysaccharides, proteins, and collagen fibers) instead of a single component. They usually have a synergistic ‘1 + 1 > 2’ effect as the property of each component is meager but the whole hybrid is of high-performance. In PSCs, for each functional layer material it requires very high purity and any impurities will significantly compromise the overall device performance. In addition, there is a limited material inventory from which the specific layer could be chosen. Making composite materials and using them as functional layers in PSCs could not only possibly reduce the cost of synthesizing high purity materials but also expand the material inventory for PSCs. However, before truly understanding the synergistic mechanisms of a composite, it will be trial and error for downselecting ‘the’ composite material.
(iii) The periodic microstructure found in nature (e.g., nanostructures on butterfly wings providing the camouflage colors and superhydrophobicity for self-cleaning; nanostructures on gecko feet providing striking attachment and detachment on walls and also giving self-cleaning, self-repair, and wear-resistant properties) is abundant. In most state-of-the-art PSCs, each layer is a simple planar layer and it is difficult to design a self-assembly process to manufacture the functional layer with a designed microstructure. Duplicating these natural microstructures with impressive performance in optical and mechanical functions is still beyond the ongoing PSC manufacturing techniques.
(iv) Nature utilizes the energy and space in a highly efficient and elegant way. PSCs still have a huge energy loss limiting their efficiency below the Shockley–Queisser (SQ) limit. The SQ limit refers to the maximum theoretical efficiency of 33.7% for a single-junction solar cell, mostly due to the spectral mismatch between the wide spectrum of sunlight and the single-bandgap of a solar cell. Thermalization (short wavelength light generated hot carrier and a fast cooling of hot carriers to release energy to heat) and in-band transparency (no absorption of long wavelength light below the bandgap) are the major losses and sum up to over 50% efficiency losses for a solar cell. In the retinas of vertebrate animals, there are different types of optical-responsive proteins in different cells that are reactive to light of different wavelengths. These individual cells are responsive to the light independently and parallelly aligned in the array to detect the monochromatic light individually from the white sunlight. In solar cells, tandem solar cells could mitigate the thermalization loss by incorporating multiple individual light absorbers in tandem stacking. As most tandem PSCs vertically stack the short-wavelength responsive cell and long-wavelength responsive cell, a parallel design of individual cells coupled by a prism to split the light might be another design for the tandem solar cell. On the other hand, coherence phenomena prevail in the biophysical system, which could lead to nontrivial optoelectronic behaviors.676 For example, the fluorescence interference pattern obtained from polydiacetylene (PDA) indicates an extraordinary microscopic coherence length for a single exciton.677 In the field of solar cells, utilizing nontrivial behaviors such as multi-excitation generation (MEG) could make it possible to break the SQ limit. One example is the singlet fission in certain organic chromophores (e.g., pentacene, rubrene, perylene) where one excited singlet state transforms into two triplet states upon close interaction with a ground-state molecule of the same kind. In this way, doubling of the external quantum efficiency (EQE) of the device in short wavelength regions to 200% is possible. By incorporating SF chromophores into solar cells, the improvement of the total exciton yield to 133%, EQE to 130%, and IQE (internal quantum efficiency) to 170% has been observed in tetracene sensitized silicon solar cells,678 pentacene incorporated organic solar cells,679 and TIPS–pentacene/PbSe QD solar cells,680 respectively. Yet these prior conceptualizations are not likely to break the Shockley–Queisser limit in the near future because of the low starting PCE point (even the tetracene sensitized silicon solar cell exhibits a low PCE of 5.1% due to the lateral structure and many technological challenges such as overall thickness control, stoichiometry and trap density678). Hybridizing the perovskite with the chromophores to trigger MEG with the aim of exceeding the SQ limit could be one promising solution, while many other issues such as interface, disordered states in organic chromophores and delicate design of an energy/electrical transduction in the system remain to be addressed.

Table Content:

Jungjin Yoon
	Dr Jungjin Yoon is a postdoctoral researcher at the Department of Materials Science and Engineering of Pennsylvania State University, United States. He obtained his PhD degree in Mechanical and Aerospace Engineering in 2018 from Seoul National University, Republic of Korea. His current research interest focuses on the scale-up manufacturing of perovskite-based solar modules and their application as portable power sources.

Yuchen Hou
	Yuchen Hou is currently a PhD student of Prof. Priya in Materials Science and Engineering at The Pennsylvania State University. He obtained his BE in Metallic Material Engineering at Qingdao University of Science & Technology in 2016 and his MS in Materials Science and Engineering at Drexel University in 2018. His current research interests include bio-photovoltaics, artificial retinas, perovskite solar cells and photodetectors, with a specific focus on integration of naturally derived biomaterials with hybrid perovskites for novel bio-optoelectronic systems and application of up-conversion materials in optoelectronics.

Neela Yennawar
	Prof. Neela Yennawar is currently the director of X-ray Crystallography and Automated Biological Calorimetry Core Facilities, Huck Institutes of the Life Sciences, Penn State. Neela received her PhD degree in Physics from the Indian Institute of Science and has been working as an Associate Research Professor in Penn State since the year of 2018. Her research interests include biological calorimetry, protein characterization, molecular modeling, X-ray crystallography, and small-angle X-ray scattering.

Mohan Sanghadasa
	Dr Mohan Sanghadasa currently leads applied research programs to develop energy storage systems, nanotechnology enhanced sensors, and photonic technologies at the U.S. Army Combat Capabilities Development Command (DEVCOM) Aviation & Missile Center (AvMC). His previous research activities at DEVCOM AvMC focused on developing polymer-based photonic devices for IFOGs in inertial measurement units. He joined the U.S. Army serving 12 years on the faculty of the University of Alabama in Huntsville (UAH). His research activities at UAH were primarily in the area of nonlinear optics. Dr Sanghadasa has also directed research on optical limiting, optical switching, and optical pattern recognition during his academic career.

Shashank Priya
	Dr Shashank Priya is currently Professor of Materials Science and Engineering at Penn State University. He is also serving as Associate Vice President for Research and Director of Strategic Initiatives in the Office of the Senior Vice President for Research (OSVPR). He is the founder and chair of the Annual Energy Harvesting Society Meeting (previously Energy Harvesting Workshop). He is also serving as the member of the Honorary Chair Committee for the International Workshop on Piezoelectric Materials and Applications (IWPMA). His research group focuses in the areas related to multifunctional materials, energy harvesting and bio-inspired systems.

Kai Wang
	Dr Kai Wang is currently Research Assistant Professor in the College of Earth and Mineral Sciences, Department of Materials Science and Engineering, Pennsylvania State University. Kai received his PhD in Polymer Engineering from The University of Akron in 2017 and joined the Center for Energy Harvesting Materials and Systems (CEHMS), Virginia Tech as a Post-doctoral Associate in the same year. His research interests include halide perovskite photovoltaics, photodetectors and photothermal applications, 2D MQW physics, and biological materials-based electronics. His current research focus is on micro-electronic sensor arrays, wireless energy transfer and innovations in manufacturing for next-generation semiconductors.

 	Fig. 2  (a) ‘Product volume’ of different PV techniques. A 3D Cartesian coordinate system is used here to quantify the market potential of different PV techniques: Efficiency, Flexibility and 1/LCOE (cost effectiveness defined by how much electricity can be used per unit cost) are the three indexes. A larger volume indicates a higher competitiveness and thus a higher market potential. (b) Power-per-weight ratios of various types of solar cells, adapted with permission from ref. 24 and 29, Copyright 2015 Springer Nature and 2019 The Royal Chemistry of Society. (c) The technique basis of perovskite PVs including high efficiency and low cost, as well as other attributes of light weight and sufficient flexibility, coupled by the potential market opportunity with a total market volume of $70 billion. Pictures are adapted with permission from ref. 29 and 30, Copyright 2019 The Royal Society of Chemistry.

Plants	Herbaceous plants	Wound closure	Seal-off and grow (production and maturation of callus parenchyma)	Cells swell, divide, and harden at the surface	
483

Secretions after injury	Latex, gum, or volatile oils are released to aid healing upon breach	Punctured cell induces a localized response	
467 and 468

Innate immune response	Pattern-recognition of microbe attack	Cellular response for specific cell response	
484

Woody plants	Bark (epidermis) and roots (rhizodermis) growth	Continual production of thick outer layers (tree bark and root bark) protecting inner layers	Continual replenishment of the hard, protective layers	
485–487

Animals	Vertebrates	Central nervous system (CNS) injury response	Glial cells in the extracellular matrix quickly form scar tissue to maintain homeostasis	Clearing of debris and rapid system stabilization	
488 and 489

Peripheral nervous system (PNS) injury	Schwann cells clear the area around the axons and allow repair	Clearing of debris and rapid system stabilization	
488 and 489

Vertebrate (hard tissue)	Bone break	Cellular response: inflammation, cartilage callus formation, lamellar bone generation and remodelling	A series of cellular activities (bone side stabilization, remodelling, mineralization, etc.)	
474

Vertebrate (soft tissue)	Wound	Cellular clotting, inflammation, proliferation, and remodelling	Cellular signalling from short-term clotting to long-term cell remodelling	
490

Stem cell response	Stem cells replicate and differentiate into target cells for new tissue growth	Adapted healing by tissue regrowth	
491 and 492

Molting (shedding) and replenishment	Replacing old and dead tissue to accommodate new growth (reptiles) or seasonally (birds)	Shedding of the outermost layer (skin, feathers, hair)	
493 and 494

Invertebrate (hard tissue)	Wound	(Insect's) endocuticle secretion from the cellular epidermis	Continual growth of the exoskeleton from the soft inner layer	
495

Exoskeleton growth and ecdysis	The organism outgrows its current exoskeleton	Periodic replacement of the newly grown exoskeleton	
496

Invertebrate (soft tissue)	Wound	Clotting (plasma protein reactions, blood cell aggregation, or cell population increase) and cellular healing	Clotting through the dense hemocyte network, hemocyte/plasma coagulation, cell reproduction	
497


 	Fig. 24  Natural examples of self-healing. (a and b) Several self-healing and defence mechanisms in herbaceous and woody plants: (a) wound closure and growth and (b) secretion cells, adapted with permission from ref. 466 and 498–500, Copyright 2005 Nature Publishing Group. (c) Schematic illustration showing the healing process in vertebrate hard tissue, adapted with permission from ref. 501, Copyright 2011 Springer Nature. (d) (i) Photograph showing the ecdysis/molting behavior of various animals, credits to multiple online sources.502–504 (e) SEM image showing the poor and defective surface of a MAPbI3 crystal. Mechanical exfoliation of top layers for obtaining a fresh surface is a commonly used method in materials research. (f) Schematic illustration showing the protection mechanism in species at different length scales: the cell wall protects the cell of the plant, skin epidermis protects the inner tissues, the outer skeleton of insects provides protection against harsh environmental stimuli, and marine collagens protect the marine creatures. In comparison, in the research of perovskite solar cells, different protection strategies have been proposed: a core–shell structure protects the core lattice, an ultrathin organic coating layer on the grain protects the core crystal of the perovskite, and a 2D capping layer can protect the 3D bulk layer underneath. Pictures are adapted with permission from online resources503,505 and from ref. 481, 506 and 507, Copyright 2020 Elsevier, 2016 The Royal Society of Chemistry, and 2021 American Chemical Society.

 	Fig. 25  Self-healing perovskite solar cell against environmental degradation. (a) (i) Photographs of perovskite films with and without PEG showing color change evolution after water-spraying. (ii) Schematic illustration of the PEG scaffold structured perovskite solar cell (PPSC). (iii) J–V curves of the PPSC before and after water spray showing the self-healing feature, adapted with permission from ref. 557, Copyright 2016 Springer. (b) (i) Schematic illustration and cross-sectional SEM image showing an inverted-structure perovskite solar cell. (ii) Normalized solar-cell parameters (VOC, JSC, FF) as a function of the proton dose, ϕ. (iii) Time dependence of the normalized solar-cell parameters after termination of the proton irradiation for a proton-irradiated device (red circles) and for a reference solar cell (black dot), adapted with permission from ref. 564, Copyright 2016 Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA. (c) (i) Schematic illustration of the biasing experiment. (ii) Schematic diagrams of the evolution of the ion distribution within the perovskite layer under solar cell working conditions, adapted wither permission from ref. 567, Copyright 2017 The Royal Society of Chemistry. (d) (i) Schematic illustration of the planar structured perovskite solar cell device. (ii) Time evolution of JSC under constant illumination and after resting the device in the dark. (iii) Schematic diagrams of the proposed photocurrent degradation and self-healing mechanism, adapted with permission from ref. 564, Copyright 2016 Springer.	 

 	Fig. 26  Self-quarantine strategies in PSCs. (a) Schematic illustration showing the different encapsulation methods (left) and the experimental procedure to assess the quantity of the toxic Pb leaked from a perovskite solar module that is damaged due to an external impact (for example, hail) (right). (b) and (c) Perovskite solar modules using different encapsulation methods (b) before and (c) after impact and water dripping test, adapted with permission from ref. 574, Copyright 2019 Springer.	 

 	Fig. 27  Bio-additives in perovskite solar cells. (a) (i) Molecular structure of caffeine. (ii) Cross-sectional SEM image of the perovskite solar cell. (iii) Thermal stability test under 85 °C continuous annealing and photovoltaic performance of the caffeine-incorporated solar cell, adapted with permission from ref. 591, Copyright 2019 Elsevier. (b) (i) Schematic illustration showing the interaction between MA+ and N-719. (ii) Photograph showing the degradation transformation process of the perovskite film with and without the N-719 additive. (iii) Stability test of the perovskite solar cell at a 60% relative humidity, adapted with permission from ref. 592, Copyright 2019 Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. (c) (i) Schematic illustration showing the interaction between DNA and the perovskite. (ii) Contact angle of a water droplet on the pristine and DNA-modified perovskites. (iii) Stability test of the perovskite solar cell with and without DNA modification at a relative humidity of 75%, adapted with permission from ref. 282, Copyright 2019 American Chemical Society. (d) (i) Molecular structure of artemisinin (ART). (ii) Schematic and cross-sectional SEM image showing the bilayer-structured perovskite solar cell induced by ART. (iii) Stability test of pristine and ART-modified perovskite solar cells at a relative humidity of 50%, adapted with permission from ref. 481, Copyright 2020 Elsevier.
